This seems to be a trend lately.

Oct 27, 2024

Several people who have been here a long time will not be renewing their subscriptions. I’m not sure what the sudden impetus is for this, I have had issues here in the past but made peace with what I still get out of it. Curious what it is now that makes folks want or need to leave.

I think I may understand a few reasons. Bear and Pika are hot right now and offer a much smaller footprint of what Micro.blog offers. Emphasis on much smaller. Some people may have reviewed what they were actually using Micro.blog for and decided that the extra stuff not on offer by Bear and/or Pika is just fine for their use case.

Others may be unhappy with the community aspect. This is where most of my issues here have taken hold. I did not join early in Micro.blog’s life so I missed some of the bigger formations of the community here. I’ve never found it easy to “find my people” and had an easier go of that on Mastodon. Funnily my community overlaps with Mastodon, so people I’ve found elsewhere I’ve also found here, or I’ve found their blogs and followed them here.

Perhaps there is also some unsettling in the direction Micro.blog is going. I’ve also been questioning this, though more in the past than now, mostly because there just hasn’t been as much talk about it lately. I’m not happy with the AI stuff at all, but I also don’t use any of it. I’m not thrilled with the tie-ins to any crypto platforms. And I’m absolutely disgusted with some of the ways some issues with outside commentary has been handled by the leadership here, either through posts, Fediverse replies, or even on some podcasts.

But I am still here. I’m not even entirely relying on some of the aspects on offer by Micro.blog that I originally was, such as cross-posting. I still find some value both in posting to my blogs here, the structure of what it offers, and to some degree, the cost, even with my very limited funds. I do still yearn for more interaction, though, my community is rather small and I go long periods without interacting with anyone else here.

If you read this and you are one of the people leaving and feel like answering my query, why are you going?

Oct 26, 2024

Know that when I can be, I am here. Know that when I stop screaming for help, I am drowning.

Renewing shock and awe.

Oct 25, 2024

I think we are all so desensitized from constant threat. From “keep calm and carry on” and “mission accomplished” and “the pandemic is over” despite what your eyes can see, your ears can hear, and your brain can witness. I realized this on the eve of Hurricane Helene. After Katrina hit Texas, I was ready to pack up my life and volunteer.

After years and years of watching kids live through school shootings and nothing changing but forcing babies to go through lockdown drills. After repeatedly destructive natural disasters, intensifying every year, wildfires, hurricanes, ice storms, flooding, and not enough being done to change our climate ways. I fear the cries about democracy degrading, about the fascist uprising, isn’t being listened to enough. Everyone is tired and tuned out unless something touches them directly. Prevention is no longer a thing, reaction is all that seems to grab attention.

Society feels broken down to the human level. Even our own internal responses are tempered by the onslaught. Shock and awe are all that sells and yet shock and awe cannot even get us to act anymore. I’m not sure where I’m going with this idea, but over the past few weeks and months, I’ve been trying to limit my exposure to most news. Not only can I personally not handle all that I cannot control, it is really dragging me down. I don’t want to be desensitized and feel helpless. I want to feel like I have agency over my life and so I think that means being more aware of my intimate surroundings.

Let’s see how this goes. I’ve already cut back greatly on social media. I’m currently on a two-week break from blogging to give my brain a break while I finish a five-session CSS workshop. I’ve started a small venture for WeblogPoMo Challenges that is very low pressure and at least keeps me interested when I think about it. For NaBloPoMo in November I’m going to add the second phase of the challenges I had in mind: an AMA where people can blog about questions they have for fellow bloggers and then other bloggers can respond with posts on their own blogs. I’m rather curious how this will go and if it will catch on. I think it could be a lot of fun.

During these last couple weeks before the election, I hope you’re all taking care of yourselves. For the last eight years I just have to check out. I cannot expose myself to the madness, it’s too much for my nervous system. 2016 broken me in ways I’ve never gotten over. Sometimes I think 2016 was the breaking point where my illness once again started backsliding. I hope we come out of this okay. I hope we don’t lose our sensitivities completely. I hope shock and awe becomes less of the norm and more a rarity that we can once again respond to.

My ME Life: Pacing

Oct 22, 2024

Maybe you’ve heard of pacing in the context of living with an energy-limiting illness like ME/CFS. Maybe you haven’t heard of pacing. If you want to understand why pacing is even a thing, you need to only think about the fact that there are no approved treatments and no cure for ME/CFS. Without pacing we would be in a constant state of crash and likely never return to our baseline ever again, perhaps increasingly worsening that baseline until we enter a more severe state of the illness. It’s as grim as it sounds.

From the #MEAction Pacing and Management Guide:

Pacing is a self-management strategy for activity. Patients who pace well are active when able, and rest when tired. They may plan extra rest ahead of strenuous activities.

Over the past two weeks I’ve been attending an online CSS workshop. I’m sitting still in a chair and listening and watching to a presenter talk about, show examples of, and parse interactions from the attendees about CSS. Sounds pretty easy, right? I’ll be quite frank here, this is what trashes my baseline the worst: sustained cognitive functions. First of all, sitting is one of the hardest things to do. Sitting for any length of time can be felt in my head pretty soon after starting. It becomes a dull and then constantly shrill pain, and can lead to migraines and increased tinnitus. Couple that with staring at a screen for hours at a time and listening to and absorbing information, it is a crash recipe in the making.

Had this workshop been five straight days of sessions, I likely wouldn’t be able to attend, even remotely in the comfort of my home. Luckily it is on Monday and Tuesday for two weeks, and then the Monday of the third week. Two and a half hours each day with minimal breaks, but broken up across three weeks. It is barely manageable. I am only able to do this because I am pacing. I’ve been sleeping 10-12+ hours every night and I’ve been limiting my other activities as much as possible.

For example, today, I’ve just finished the morning workshop session and I have two other taxing tasks I’d like to complete, but I can only realistically do one. The other one will have to wait until tomorrow or Thursday. What are these other tasks? Surely some gigantic undertaking, right? Well, no. One of them is baking something from scratch, the other is making a phone call that will require a series of back and forth discussions with another person about an intense subject. Both of these are giant tasks for someone with ME/CFS.

I have been waiting to bake something for three days, my butter and eggs have been sitting on the counter since Sunday. I will choose to do that over the phone call, and that is the only remaining thing I will get done today. If I finish it and rest, maybe I’ll be able to do one smaller thing before I go to bed, but I probably won’t push it. The thing about crashes is that they usually are delayed by 12+ hours, so I could think I’m in the clear and then do another thing only to have a worsened crash tomorrow night into Thursday than if I didn’t do anything else. A lot of times it is a waiting game to see how my body reacts and what other external factors might happen out of my control.

Pacing is imperfect and even after more than 30 years of practice, I still get it wrong. But it is one of the only tools I have available to live my life and get done what I need to get done, even if on an extended or delayed timeline. I’ve probably talked about this before, I tend to “plan” at the week or month stage rather than daily. I set out what I think I need to get done in a week, and that often extends to the following week, but can usually be wrapped up within the month. Imperfect but essential.

I haven't blogged in more than a week.

üå± Garden Status (2024/10/16)

Oct 16, 2024

Late season garden status! There are still beautiful things to see in the garden even in mid-October. Today, the irrigation was shut down and the system blown out. We also had our first real fall rain last night. Nothing blustery or stormy, it was rather nice, actually. I slept with the window open and it was a lovely lullaby that started sometime during the night. I rather wanted to stay in bed this morning.

But I ventured out into the garden and took just a few pictures to share. I will be mowing the lawn Friday or Saturday, it is quite long. But that is to be expected after overseeding and fertilizing a couple weeks ago. This might be the last or second to the last mow of the year. I can’t remember if I’ve mowed in November before. Still several things blooming, including some spring blooming plants that have lost their minds.

The Japanese maple trees on the patio are just starting to get some color. The two Bloodgoods on the street are not yet changing. The magnolia tree has some monster fruit this year which the squirrels love to chew on and eat the bright red-orange seeds when they split apart. I’ve already changed out the patio containers to pansies and violas and some ornamental kale/cabbage. I ended up keeping four bacopa and some Silverdust dusty miller. I’ll get some photos once they fill in a bit more.

This Mexican Snowball echeveria will soon be moved under cover either on the patio or front porch. Though it should be hardy enough here it will not tolerate the long stretches of wet weather all winter in Portland.

I bought several succulents and sedums this year. Of the two ground cover sedums this one is my favorite with the sweet rosettes with little notched crenelations on the margins of the leaves. The picture doesn’t do the water drop “jewels” justice as it appeared bedazzled in the light today.

Happy to see this transplanted catmint has made so much progress since I moved it to the front yard about six weeks ago. It has put on all new foliage growth, it’s like an entirely new plant, and is so pleased with its location I’m going to get a late season flush of blooms. The bees and hummingbirds will be happy.

The last of the late blooming sedums in the garden. This lovely variegated variety is covered in buds that are just opening up to bright pink starry blooms. Lovely in the low light this morning.

Taken for granted or outright overlooked.

Oct 15, 2024

Either way, it isn’t great. A community of disabled folks I am surrounded by on the disabled.social Mastodon instance has been crying out loud about this since at least last year. Many people I am regularly connected with have already decided they will not vote. This doesn’t even take into account the ones that find it difficult to vote due to barriers in the voting process. This is just people who don’t feel represented by either candidate.

I think the Democrats take us for granted. They know the Republicans really don’t care about anyone but themselves. The Democrats think disabled voters will choose them because without them there won’t even be the tragically underfunded and difficult labyrinth of social services that there is now. But it is worth reading some of the quotes from this piece. It’s a bigger problem than either candidate is willing to face. And while Democrats are still busy chasing the middle, they’re losing some of their most progressive voters who just happened to be disabled.

A new report from Rutgers University estimates that about 40.2 million eligible voters in the quickly approaching U.S. presidential election are disabled. Add those who cohabitate with people who have a disability, and you‚Äôre looking at close to one-third of the voting population for an election in which health care is among the key campaign issues.

The disabled voting bloc is growing as the U.S. population ages, but voters and advocates say the hurdles that make people feel excluded from the electoral process aren‚Äôt being addressed. That ranges from inaccessible campaign materials to former President Donald Trump and Vice President Kamala Harris seldomly mentioning how issues like COVID-19 impact the disability community, as well as Trump making a statement at a rally last month that advocacy groups considered discriminatory.

Matt Mills is a former Trump supporter and avid voter. The 41-year-old from Brown County in southern Ohio has lupus, polyneuropathy and received two kidney transplants since 2017.

This year, he‚Äôll be voting for Harris because of her ‚Äúproven track record‚Äù of advocating for health care policies he likes. Even so, he believes voters with disabilities are taken for granted.

‚ÄúWhen we don‚Äôt see ourselves represented,‚Äù he said, ‚Äúthen we continue to get beat down and we get tired and we get discouraged.‚Äù

There‚Äôs more to engaging disabled voters, said Meighan Stone, who uses a wheelchair due to symptoms of long COVID.

‚ÄúThe focus on ableist jokes or whether or not disabled people are visibly at campaign events reduces disabled people to one thing,‚Äù Stone said, adding, ‚Äú… That‚Äôs a level of discrimination we live with every day.‚Äù

Stone would like to see both candidates focus on solving how long it takes to get disability benefits ‚Äî about 6-8 months, according to the federal government ‚Äî and getting the Food and Drug Administration to approve a treatment for long COVID, saying, ‚ÄúI think both campaigns want to act as if they solved a pandemic and it‚Äôs over.‚Äù

Day two of a CSS workshop.

Oct 15, 2024

A little while back I wrote a post about more thoughts on community in which I mention that I did a spur-of-the-moment crowdfund to attend an online CSS workshop. The workshop started yesterday at 9am PT and ran until 11:30am. The same for today, and Monday and Tuesday next week, and the Monday of the week after.

I did fine yesterday, but I had a bunch of other stuff I needed to get done. I also started the day rather frantically deciding to reinstall the Zoom workspace app after removing it following all the AI crap that got added. The day felt like a marathon, though the session itself was doable.

Today felt entirely different. I struggled to be coherent this morning and I faded about 45 minutes before the session ended. The information today was a lot and I’m really glad I have access to the recording to not only rewatch at my own pace, but to pause when I want to look something up and take a moment to process the information, and possibly play around with some experiments. This would be a perfect example of what I want to use Tiny Pages for, so maybe I can start getting some posts in place over there with the experiments, even if the site has had no customizations added to it yet.

Overall I am really pleased with the level of information and concepts we are being exposed to in this workshop. It’s my first Smashing workshop and I wasn’t quite sure of the level of experience it would cater to. In the case of this one, I already feel like it is worthwhile. I also think I am learning some really great groundwork for helping me get started on CSS for People if and when I get started on that.

Setting some blogging guidelines.

Oct 4, 2024

This post probably isn’t necessary but I started to reply to someone on Mastodon and instead decided to put this little guide here on how I see myself posting across my various blogs at this point. I’ve been doing it long enough now that I no longer really have to think about it when something comes into my head, I usually know what blog it needs to go on.

This might also serve as a slight update to my types of blog posts piece as I find that “types” are not so much the types listed there as much as the brevity or intention of the post. In Drupal-speak, this would be “content types.” IYKYK.

3x5.pics

Note post

This is the only post type on 3x5.pics, includes a short one line title that acts as the first sentence of the notes and the content of the note itself as body text. These are very short posts, index card sized in style and length.

Microblog and 100 Days of Blog

Bookmark + quote post

I use this type quite a bit for health and chronic illness related posts. I will use the MB bookmarking feature to bookmark the post, article, video, etc, and then include an embed in a post with a relevant quote. This is usually a short sub-300 character, titleless post.

Photo blog post

These are mostly posted on my main microblog as a featured photo but sometimes will be more than one photo that I share for something else. If featured and only includes a single photo, usually sub-300 characters and titleless. Photos are rare on 100 Days of Blog and only used to support a Title + content post. Sometimes this post type is cross-posted.

Title + content post

A Title + content post is your standard blog post with title and, importantly, for theming purposes on both my main microblog and 100 Days of Blog, a summary first paragraph that gets included in the paginated list pages. Sometimes cross-posted.

Titleless post

Short and timely, like a tweet or toot, these will fall under the 300 word boundary. I post these only to my main microblog and will choose to post there instead of one of my four “themed” Mastodon accounts when it is something that could cross-over to more than one of them. I can cross-post to one of the Mastodon accounts.

Table for One Cafe

Recipe experiment post

Since nothing exists of this site yet, I envision this being the only post type. If it is not self-explanatory already, in fact the entire point of the site, it will be recipes that I adapt and experiment with from a banker’s box of old recipes I inherited from my grandmother.

The format will be more elaborate than the Recipe posts I publish on weblog. There will likely be the original recipe, perhaps a scanned image from where it originated, a back story (if there is one), my experimentation, and any results I land on.

Themes/Styles/Custom

Title + content post

All posts on themes.lol will have a title with content and a summary in the post frontmatter/meta data for archive and listings.

Weblog

Photo story post

This post type does not exist yet on weblog, but is long overdue. I started working on image layouts last year when I first set up weblog and its theme. I quickly abandoned it as I set in to create the whole of the Styleguide before doing much else. This type of post will greatly reflect the style of posts I was publishing on my Substack newsletter. It will be photo heavy, use some specific image layouts, and include an accompanying story or information relevant to the images.

Linklist post

I recently created the template for this post type on weblog. Linklists are currently either bookmarks or blogroll spins/postrolls.

Recipe post

A more standard recipe post, usually someone else’s recipe which I have made so many times with my own customizations that I want to put it somewhere that I have bookmarked, like my blog!

Title + content post

This post type makes up the bulk of content on weblog and is similar in nature to the post of the same time listed above for my main microblog and 100 Days of Blog. Though summary text for archive or featured post listing is added via frontmatter/meta data and not within the post itself via the first paragraph.

If I have missed anything here, this post may update, as I may later use it for reference.

Thinking more on community.

Sep 25, 2024

Last week, for the first time ever, I put up a post on Mastodon with a link to my Buy me a coffee page and a request for help. The post was a couple replies down in a thread because the original post was simply “I wish I could do this CSS workshop but I’d have to ask for donations” or something like that.

I’ve never put out a mutual aid request for a number of reasons. First, asking for help, any kind of help, is hard to do. People go through years of therapy to learn how to ask for help. Getting denied help in the past can dissuade you from asking for help in the future. There is a connotation that asking for help is a sign of weakness. Asking for help can open you up to abuse, harm, and fraudulent behavior. The exchange of money changes relationships, sometimes for the worse. And probably most of all for me I dislike asking for something I’m not sure I can repay.

But after the post I got a private message from someone who said they’d be willing to chip in. It was someone I trusted and felt that I wouldn’t be harmed by if I accepted the offer. After that exchange I decided to put the link up to see if anyone else would help as well. I really didn’t expect much, I didn’t expect three more people in quick succession to donate, and I absolutely did not expect a final person to pay the balance of what I needed to attend the workshop.

It was an extremely humbling experience. All of these people are mutuals on Mastodon and I occasionally interact with them. They all provide enjoyment to my timeline and I’d like to think that I too provide them with something in theirs. But I don’t think I would have pegged any of them on the “community” level of support or that we know each other well enough for that. How can I say that better? I would have thought others I am connected to to have helped out before many of these people and it is making me question and take a fresh look at many things.

I have talked about community many times. Lots of people talk about community. I’m not sure any of us talk about it with the same goals or intentions. I’ve tried to define what it means to me and how I identify it “in the wild” and time and again the assumptions I made or positions I took seem to become irrelevant in the face of new facts or circumstances.

Community must evolve, it cannot remain stagnant, and that might mean reevaluating where you decide to take community. What are the terms you take it on? Different places within the same community can have different feelings and behaviors. The same person in one place may seem foreign in another. These are things I am considering right now.

I hope to come back to this soon with some clarifications to my community “non-negotiables” posted at the end of this weblog post.

previous to the pandemic, the prevalence of ME/CFS in the US was estimated to be about 3 million people

since the pandemic started, about 5.3% of Covid infections result in Long Covid, equalling about 15 million people (adults)

roughly 50% of Long Covid cases meet the diagnostic criteria for ME/CFS

so today, in 2024, about 10 million US adults have ME/CFS and there are ZERO approved treatments and funding for research is among the lowest of all diseases of this magnitude (QoL, illness burden, etc)

she compares: 6.7 million Alzheimers patients in the US have 5 approved FDA drugs; 1 million MS patients have access to at least 20 drugs approved by the FDA

it’s just so unacceptable to have lived more than 30 years with this illness and seen nothing fucking changing¬†

well, one thing is changing, the numbers of affected are increasing due to Covid

Whenever I get a squirrel in my brain.

Sep 6, 2024

A post idea, I start a new Draft. I have many that just sit there and I never finish. I have a list on weblog of all of them, I try to keep it updated, and I cross them off if I finish them, adding a permalink to the published post or page. I wonder if I should go back and write something for the ones that may never really be done as first envisioned?

I could sort of talk through what I had been thinking and at least “put a pin in it” and make it serve more as a “digital garden” piece. Hmm, I think I like this idea. I’ve talked about wanting weblog to be more of a digital garden before. I could provide a tag for these post types. I could even come up with a different template if I decided I wanted to differentiate them even more.

Today I have an idea for a piece about success in response to someone else’s blog post about success. I think I’d like to write about what success means to me. How that meaning can change, is never static, and does not equate to “progress”. The idea of progress as success is the same misnomer as thinking the economy can forever expand. That expansion for the sake of expansion without consideration of damage to other inputs is not actually progress.

But I think it could be even more personal than that. This person was pointing out that folks with mental health issues or depression might actually consider getting out of bed in the morning success. That got me thinking about how my measure of success has decreased over time, tied to my chronic illness. Success with chronic illness is a subject that contains multitudes. How much I’d go into that might serve for launching a series.

I’ve yet to write a “response” post and use the appropriate microformats, so if I decide to this, of course I will take it as an opportunity to use the right markup. Lots of opportunities just in these two ideas: a new “digital garden” post type, with tag, and possibly a new template (TBD); and a response post, possibly also requiring a tag and template.

Viral infections are not beneficial.

Four main categories of pathogens that humans deal with are viruses, bacteria, fungi, and parasites. The evidence for pathogens that may be beneficial to the immune system is almost entirely for parasitic worms and friendly (commensal) bacteria. In contrast, many viruses can even trigger the onset of autoimmune diseases or allergies.

üå± Garden Status (2024/08/21)

Aug 21, 2024

I haven’t done a garden status in quite a while. I do not tend to get out into the garden as much during the height of summer when it is just too hot and sunny for my liking. When I do get out there it is to finish a specific task early or later in the day and then I’m back inside or resting on the shaded patio.

We have a brief hiatus in heat this week and may actually have a few days of rain. The garden is starting to go into “late summer” mode when some of the later blooming plants get going, I’ve been trying to add more of these over the last several years. When the garden was younger, I had mostly spring blooming plants and that left a dearth of blooms during the summer months. Now I have more going on later, whether it be perennials that push a second (or third) flush, or plants that just do not start blooming until August and then go until frost.

Starting with an agapanthus that has been rather disappointing since I planted it. It’s probably my fault. Agapanthus love to be root bound, and I only bought one plant and then divided it immediately. The first year I only got one bloom head on one of the halves. The second year I only got one sparse bloom head on the other half. Last year I got no blooms at all. This year I dug one of the halves out and am going to put it into a container and compare how the two halves perform from here on out. Here is the larger of the two halves, finally putting on some size and pushing a nice bloom head this year.

August 14, 2011

Not so fun with smoke detectors.

Aug 15, 2024

I just went around changing six smoke detector batteries and backup batteries because one of them randomly went off and I couldn’t tell which one it was. I’m in the habit of changing them all at the same time any time I need to replace the batteries in one so that I always know how old they all are.

The worst part about this task is that you’re up on a step stool or ladder so you can reach the dang things, then you need a hand to push the test button, so you only have one free hand to plug one ear, and then you end up deaf and ringing in the other ear for the next hour or two.

Now marking on the calendar this date so I in six months I can probably do this all over again. Four of them are the same and are not hard-wired, but I had to replace them all a couple years ago because they all expired, which is still a weird thing to me. I understand there are compounds that age and break down inside of them. This also means you can’t just toss them in the trash. The two upstairs are hard-wired so they only have a back-up battery in them. Luckily they all have the same little access panel that slides over to replace the batteries without having to take them off their mounts.

I’d really love to be able to use the Eve Home smoke detectors because then I’d have information about them in my Home app. I’d be able to see the status of the batteries and probably when they were last changed. I assume I would also get a notice on my phone or other device letting me know when it is time to change the batteries or which one is going off in the case one goes off. But for some reason they are not available in the US, only Europe.

Aug 15, 2024

They don’t see how “there’s always been viruses, why is this time such a big deal?” or “you weren’t living like a recluse before!”

It’s because this time is different. I won’t stop sharing this stuff. It’s the fight of my life.

There have always been viruses, and there have always been immunocompromised people, but prior to COVID there was never this level of viral spread, with a virus of this severity, with this ability to reinfect, with this level of transmissibility. Immunocompromised people are under constant siege, with levels of disease unseen in modern times being normalized in all public spaces.

For many disabled and immunocompromised people, segregation is as complete as their need to avoid COVID. Indoor dining, of course, is out. Going to bars, happy hours, work events, weddings, conferences, parties, anything where large groups of people gather is a risk, even in a high-quality mask which will leave you unable to eat, drink, and a likely target for snide remarks and mocking jokes. Because again, the political messaging coming from the top encourages healthy people not only not to protect vulnerable people, but to mock and deride them."

August 2, 2019

My ME Life: Lessons learned from Shingles

Aug 10, 2024

Today’s entry in this series‚Äîit has been a while‚Äîhas me remembering that time I had Shingles. The reason this comes up now is several-fold: many people are seeing themselves having Shingles right now following an initial or repeat Covid infection; I similarly had Shingles during some of my sickest years with ME/CFS prior to a remission; and it is an important lesson in mild or asymptomatic infection still leading to evidence of viral persistence and the long tail of chronic vs acute illness.

I was in my early- to mid-20s, definitely not what is the expected age for Shingles. In fact, today you can’t even get a Shingles vaccine until you are over 50 (unless approved by a doctor). Shingles is caused by the same virus as Chicken Pox. If you had Chicken Pox as a child you have this virus still residing in your body. This is what viruses do. Many common (and not so common) viruses take up residence in reservoirs and tissue and lie in wait to be reactivated later for any number of reasons, usually an additional stress or illness, and then can cause a whole new slew of terrible and more damaging illness than the acute stage of the virus. That is exactly what Long Covid is, by the way.

Very often the acute state of a viral infection can be mild or asymptomatic. In my case, my sister, a few years older than me, had Chicken Pox. My parents, like many other parents in a wave of copy-cat like behavior, were led to believe the right thing to do was put us together “to get it over with” so we would both be sick at the same time. I never responded with a symptomatic infection. I never had a breakout of pox/bumps and the associated symptoms of Chicken Pox. Did I have Chicken Pox? Well, yes, because two decades later I had Shingles.

The lesson here is that even with a mild or asymptomatic viral infection, you can still end up with a chronic condition from that virus. Likewise, you can have a “mild” or asymptomatic Covid infection and still end up chronically ill and disabled with Long Covid. Many asymptomatic infections are a result of your immune system being unable to mount a response, where your body may already be too sick or damaged to show signs of an infection. That doesn’t mean you aren’t sick, it likely means more damage, unseen, is occurring within your body.

My case of Shingles was considered out of the ordinary for someone who appeared to be well, yet with a chronic illness, ME/CFS. Up until the last ten years or so, ME/CFS was still widely ignored and refuted by medical professionals. Some of this still persists today and is being taken up again in the fight to recognize Long Covid. It took me around five years to get any sort of answer about my condition and be told I have ME/CFS. Even afterwards I was psychologized, told to push through it, that the will to get “better” was in my head and my own fault. With Shingles too I was first told it was a rash, even when I explained the terrible pain I was in.

Shingles, if untreated, can damage nerves, internal organs, and cause blindness if on the face. This is why it can appear to “wrap around” your body. For me it was on the side of my torso and wrapped around the front to my belly. To this day, I still have lingering pain sometimes and wonder if there is internal damage due to the length of time it took for my doctor to recognize what it was and agree to treat me. Treatment is an anti-viral. If that sounds familiar, that may be because acute treatment of Covid is also an anti-viral. They work to stop viral replication from occurring inside the body. Years ago, and perhaps still today, when I had Shingles, many anti-virals were used to treat common sexually transmitted viruses.

While I had insurance through a state-run program, the list of approved medicine did not cover these anti-virals specifically because of the stigma associated with sexually transmitted viruses. It was like the boards, likely mostly men, sitting around deciding what medication they deemed “appropriate” for people to take should include methods to shame people when they acquired illnesses of “ill repute.”

I was a young chronically ill person who had already been mistreated and gaslighted so much by the medical profession and now I faced paying $600 out of pocket for an anti-viral to protect my nerves and organs from damage. Is it any wonder I don’t trust doctors and healthcare even to this day? They haven’t gotten any better looking at how the pandemic is being handled. Depending on the calculator you use, $600 then is over $1100 today, and medication is far more expensive now. I have been seeing people with insurance coverage paying $900 out of pocket for Paxlovid.

My ME/CFS was also caused by a viral infection, likely Epstein‚ÄìBarr virus (EBV), the virus that causes Mononucleosis in the acute stage. Many people with Long Covid go on to qualify for the diagnostic criteria of ME/CFS. These post-viral illnesses all act similarly: an acute viral illness followed by some period of time and then another health trigger, either stress or another illness, often viral, that reactivates a virus-in-waiting in the body months or years later, launching the chronic illness stage.

I’ve been watching this happen on repeat for so many years now I have started to see patterns. Following my anti-viral treatment for Shingles I entered a remission in my ME/CFS. No one at the time made any connection, and frankly neither did I. But now I have been reading studies and literature about Long Covid and additional related post-viral illness, and anti-virals can play a key role in these conditions.

Aug 9, 2024

The cumulative global incidence of long COVID is around 400 million individuals, which is estimated to have an annual economic impact of approximately $1 trillion‚Äîequivalent to about 1% of the global economy. Several mechanistic pathways are implicated in long COVID, including viral persistence, immune dysregulation, mitochondrial dysfunction, complement dysregulation, endothelial inflammation and microbiome dysbiosis.

It was a lovely cool morning in the garden today. Really glad I got out there first thing to do some watering and puttering. A song sparrow hopped around keeping me company the whole time. I think it was waiting for me to refill the water basin (aka bird bath).

Feeling bad.

Jul 16, 2024

Why do I allow myself to feel bad for feeling how I feel? I feel bad that I feel bad, but also that someone else might also feel bad that I feel bad. It’s all bad. It’s so bad that I just want to not feel bad and pretend I don’t, when I do. I just need to process it for the time it takes.

I’m really tired tonight. I’m in what we call a “tired but wired” phase pre-crash. My brain won’t shut up but I’m basically slobbering on myself from exhaustion. I’m still feeling bad about the other stuff that has happened this week. I’ve got so much to do before the weekend. The final step, it better be the final step, of the neighbor’s tree falling on my garage is replacing the fence between their backyard and my driveway and garage. That’s happening at the end of this week.

To prepare for this, I need to move some masonry blocks and some mulch. The mulch pile is left over from this spring when I had a very large load delivered. I spread it all but a couple wheelbarrow loads to top up areas that degrade faster from irrigation and that need topping up after planting or moving plants.

I think tomorrow I’m going to vacuum my car. I left it out of the garage tonight so that after I’m done vacuuming I can load up the wheelbarrow and move at least one load and then leave the rest in the wheelbarrow until the fence is done. That way I can get everything out of the way of the fence construction and nothing is on my driveway.

I think there is something in the idea of imposter syndrome that correlates to emotional invalidation. Like emotional invalidation leads to a personal imposter syndrome that prevents you from relying on and believing your own emotions and feelings. After reading the post I linked to and mentioned in my 100 Days of Blog post this morning, I thought more about it and how it mentions the long-term psychological damage that can cause a person subjected to emotional invalidation repeatedly or especially as a child to have issues acknowledging and working through their own emotions.

It makes me think about my time as a teenager when I was denied the validation for my illness for years. How my mother and I travelled all over trying to find out why I was sick. And how even after we had a diagnosis I was subjected to psychological harm via doctors continuing to treat me like it was in my head and that I brought it on myself. I start to cry now when I think about this, but I’m also crying for myself today. I’ve got a lot of trauma and for a long time I buried it and just tried to survive but I can’t deny it anymore because it’s just me. Nobody else is going to save me.

üå± Garden Status (2024/07/16)

Jul 16, 2024

This garden status is dedicated to finally getting stuck into planting three of my new plants. This order of six plants from Annie’s Annuals and Perennials came during the heatwave nearly two weeks ago and I’ve been trying to get out to put the succulents in the ground but have been repeatedly delayed. Today, I did it!

Now, they won’t look like much yet, but eventually the two put into the ground will be nice creeping groundcovers that bloom with little star flowers, as most succulents do. I decided to put the Mexican Snowball in a pot and set it in the garden, a style borrowed from my favorite succulent lady, Laura Eubanks of Design for Serenity. This will allow me to remove the pot from the garden during the rainiest part of the winter; although the echeveria is hardy in my zone, it will not survive cold and persistently wet conditions in slow draining soil, which I have with my SE Portland clay soil.

I'm frustrated here.

Jul 10, 2024

In complete honesty: I’m concerned at the longevity of this platform. It does not feel like the actual stability and resolution of issues takes precedence over adding new shiny features. There does not appear to be enough support for reviewing help center posts. Downtime is noticeably a concern of mine now, at least seven or eight times this year so far, that I’ve noticed. I’m not entirely sure the pros outweigh the cons anymore.

Moving elsewhere was on my mind recently while writing answers to interview questions about my blog(s). I’m not entirely sure where I want to go or if all blogs should move together. I’d still like to see how Neato progresses, if it progresses. And with my health in limbo, I don’t really have the bandwidth to do anything right away. I’d most likely need to wait until this fall or winter.

And there is just the lack of “community” aspect that I have griped about before. I was never really here for the community, but if you’re going to claim it as a feature, than you bloody well better maintain the community. I’m not sure I buy the “transition” period excuse, as it seems it just isn’t a priority. Making a community isn’t a side gig you fit in when you have time. This feels similar to excuses by tech companies for their lack of ability to acquire and maintain diverse teams.

Finally, there is the AI stuff and a general feeling of “ick” I get from some of the proselytizing around here. I’m not going to get into it deeply right now, there is plenty history on this blog of me talking about it. Every day we find out more about how this tech is a solution in search of a problem, spreading bad information, continuing to steal our intellectual property, causing harm to marginalized groups, and above all else contributing to the rampant climate change that leads to heatwaves like the one shredding my health right now. AI companies are Bad Actors who do not want to play by any rules. They want to run roughshod over nuance and wear us down with apathy. I don’t want to be contributing to their businesses at all and even if I am not using the parts that have been integrated here, I feel extremely dirty and unsettled about it.

The staunch upper ridge in the western U.S. will sustain an intense, widespread and long duration heat wave for the West Coast. Widespread temperature records are expected to be tied or broken with highs in the upper 90s to 110s likely up and down the West Coast and portions of the Great Basin. Humans are doing frightening stuff.

Jul 7, 2024

Some extremely scary trends related to the continued run of Covid-19, new strains, and research ties to both Long Covid and ME/CFS this week. You would do everything possible to prevent this from happening to yourself or someone you love if you truly understood what it is like to live this illness.

I have a hard time with so much these days. But top of the pile is the way society just throws our health away for some short-term idea of “freedom” and “the economy.” Several pieces I have read recently suggest that if we continue down this path most US adults will end up with some form of Long Covid, and believe me, there will be no economy when that happens.

Altogether, multiple pathways that deal with oxidative stress (glutathione, superoxide dismutase, lipid oxidative damage, etc.) appear to have been overwhelmed in the immune cells in both ME/CFS and long-COVID patients. In all cases, the ME/CFS patients were worse off than the long-COVID patients ‚Äì leading the authors to suggest that long COVID was an intermediate condition between health and ME/CFS.

The Human Toll When Inequality and Disease Collide

Having spent a ground-breaking career studying the racialization, policing, and criminalization of HIV, Dr. Thrasher has come to understand a deeper truth at the heart of our society: that there are vast inequalities in who is able to survive viruses and that the ways in which viruses spread, kill, and take their toll are much more dependent on social structures than they are on biology alone.

No, thank you.

Jul 6, 2024

These types of posts are not even taking into account existing chronic illness, disability, or comorbidity. This just assumes you are a relatively healthy person, up-to-date on vaccination and booster, and don’t have many vices.

“I would argue that COVID-19 is not a disease of the lungs at all,” she said. “It seems most likely that it is what we call a vascular and neurologic infection, affecting both nerve endings and our cardiovascular system.”

It’s no surprise that experts say SARS-CoV-2 ‚Äì the name of the virus that causes COVID-19 ‚Äì is complex, with many of its pathways just beginning to be understood. But some things are becoming clear. One of the best reviews of long COVID symptoms, Viswanathan said, appeared last January in Nature Reviews Microbiology. It detailed the disease’s effects throughout the body, including the pancreas, blood vessels and reproductive system.

“SARS-CoV-2 is excellent at triggering your immune system to go from zero to 100,” said Dr. Lindsay McAlpine, a neurologist who is director of the Yale NeuroCovid Clinic in New Haven, Connecticut. That revving of the immune response leads to both a “wide swath of inflammation” and excessive blood clotting, she said.

“Perhaps the viral replication is going on in the lungs and nasopharynx (the area at the top of the throat that connects the nose to the respiratory system). But the inflammation that the virus triggers is systemic,” McAlpine said.

I think we need a way to move a post from one blog to another.

Jun 28, 2024

I was authoring a post on 100 Days of Blog, and it said so right above the web editor (ie “Post to your blog: 100daysof.blog”) but after saving it showed up on this blog somehow. I don’t really understand why that happened. I did navigate on another tab to a micro.anniegreens.lol admin page, but to have that change the currently in-progress blog post’s target is a bit disconcerting.

So it seems there should be a way to move a post from one blog to another from within the edit form after a post is already published. In this case I had to delete the post from the incorrect blog, copying the content first, and re-creating it on the correct blog. I’m not sure what the technical limitations of this ask would be, but it sure would be a useful improvement for premium accounts with multiple blogs.

This is the first time I’ve had this happen, where a post ends up on the wrong blog, either by my own accident or by whatever happened this time. But I could absolutely see it being a problem the more blogs I have. As I am only currently authoring on two blogs, but I have a third one in the works, it could get ugly.

Update: I just discovered even more weirdness with this! Whatever happened in the “cross-over” of this piece of content in-progress from one blog to the other added a new category that was not previously here! This is some funkiness and I’m considering filing something in the help center, though two of the last few things I’ve posted have not gotten a single response, so I’m not sure how much attention issues actually get there.

Quote from reader response to STAT article.

Jun 23, 2024

I don’t recall this piece in STAT when it came out, but I’m reading it now following feedback from a reader. Emphasis mine.

I agree with the authors that NIH needs reform. I was an athletic, otherwise healthy person who was struck down and disabled by long Covid in January 2022. For over two years, I‚Äôve watched life pass me by as NIH has fumbled the $1.15 billion allocated to it by Congress to study and treat long Covid. This initiative, known as RECOVER, has failed to publish any research that furthers our understanding of the underlying cause of long Covid and the vast majority of clinical trials they‚Äôve launched are for drugs that people have already tried and found unhelpful.

Perhaps NIH would not have bungled the long Covid funding had it not completely ignored other post viral diseases, namely myalgic encephalomyelitis (ME) for the past 40 years. ME receives only $15 million a year ‚Äî the most underfunded disease per patient burden at the NIH.

NIH should reevaluate how it allocates funding to diseases and base allocations on objective patient burdens. HIV, a disease with treatments that allow people with it to live mostly normal lives, receives $3.3 billion annually through NIH. Meanwhile, ME patients are disabled, have no approved treatments, and suffer a higher patient burden. Covid long haulers are suffering the same fate, many struck down as first wavers in March of 2020 are still disabled and sick as ever over four years later. Despite this, there is still no yearly allocation for long Covid in NIH‚Äôs baseline budget, as funding has only ever come from one time appropriations. NIH should right-size funding for ME and long Covid and start taking these diseases seriously.

I hope I word this okay.

Jun 19, 2024

And I hope it doesn’t come off sounding like I’m unappreciative of the work that goes into Tiny Theme. I am very appreciative of it, and in fact, I reveal the attribution to the creator within the footer of my microblog, along with references to my customization.

However, today, while adding a new head.html template override in order to add a <meta name="color-scheme" content="light dark"> for passing through to an <iframe> also using the same meta tag, I discovered that the Tiny Theme head.html adds a <link rel="author" href="humans.txt"> which functions to say that the author of the current document is what is in that humans.txt. That is, the creator of Tiny Theme.

While it is true that the theme was authored by this person, the current document was not. I do not think this is the appropriate use of the rel="author" attribute, either via link or meta tags. I’d venture to guess most people don’t realize that is there.

From MDN:

author

Indicates the referenced document provides further information about the author of the current document or article. Relevant for <link>, <a>, and <area> elements.

With <a> and <area>, it indicates the linked document (or mailto:) provides information about the author of the nearest <article> ancestor if there is one, otherwise the entire document.

With <link>, it represents the author of the entire document.

Update: I filed an issue on the repo: Usage of link rel=author in head.html

üå± Garden Status (2024/06/15)

Jun 15, 2024

We are five days from the summer solstice. Today’s sunset was at 9:01pm and next Thursday will be the latest sunset of the year at 9:02pm. I read today that the summer solstice in Portland is 6 hours and 59 minutes of daylight longer than the winter solstice. Nearly a whole new day of light within a single day. I’m exhausted just thinking about it. I love what summer does to the garden, but I do get weary this time of year and dream just a little bit of a cooler and darker existence, when the rains come.

This update includes some random shots from the garden this morning. The back sun loving perennial border is coming into full bloom now. Finally, the Shasta daisies are opening, the two I have are double and full of frills. This buttery yellow one is so smooth and creamy I could plant it everywhere and not get tired of it. We had some rain today and the centers gathered the rainfall and a fly was taking a drink, I could not decide on a favorite so you get all three.

Along the top of the rockery, and to be honest through the rockery, the blue star creeper is…creeping. This plant is a thug and I have to tear bunches of it out every year but I just cannot tear it out completely. Not only because it would be so hard but because look at it, it creates this mounding cascade of blue pillows this time of year and is usually covered in bees. Please note: this was one four-inch nursery container when I planted it üëÄ‚Äîwatch out!

The back perennial border is mostly full of sun loving plants that flower from now until fall, with the exception of the far west corner that falls into shade against the west fence in the afternoon. But the vista of flower colors and foliage is always lovely and calming in the morning when they all see sun together.

A single container update, as this is turning into my favorite combination so far, purple + orange. I’ve been calling this begonia orange creamsicle though I know that is not what it is called. The purple flower is a Jamesbrittenia hybrid and is performing far better than I imagined it would in its mostly shady location on the patio. Love the color of the flowers with orange center playing off the orange of the begonia. Gorgeous.

On Accountability

Jun 13, 2024

We had a bit of drama in an overlapping of two communities I am part of a couple weeks ago. I think it went by mostly unnoticed by the folks who were not affected or who did not involve themselves in the commenting/replies.

This post by Mandy today touches on something I think is important. Though the entire post is more about accountability in the sense of roles or positions at a job, she explains something about it that is absolutely relevant to how several people responded to what happened in our communities.

If you‚Äôve ever participated in a blameless post-mortem, you‚Äôve already experienced that model of accountability. In a post-mortem, the aim is to unearth what happened‚Äîthe story‚Äîwithout passing judgment on or retaliating against any of the characters within it. The explicit freedom from retribution is what makes it possible for people to speak openly and honestly of an event. If I can speak candidly about how I fucked up, knowing no harm will come to me, I am more able to admit my mistakes, learn from them, and commit to making whatever repairs may be necessary to recover from the harm. Paradoxically, it‚Äôs the lack of blame that creates the possibilities of repair and insight, while the threat of punishment forecloses opportunities for both.

I think the key line here is: “The explicit freedom from retribution is what makes it possible for people to speak openly and honestly of an event.” And unfortunately that did not happen in this case, because almost immediately “one side” started to call people names and act like a victim. The person on the other side who attempted to start a conversation and “speak candidly about how [someone] fucked up” was then made out to be the bad guy. So all good intentions and good will was out the door at that point.

At this point I’m not entirely sure how this could have been made better. But Mandy’s framing of accountability from an old definition of “to render an account” is a good place to start. How can we put our pitchforks down have rational discussions through stories? In fact, the actual harmed person did just that on their own blog, and they were still further attacked by the other side. It was rather shameful and I’m sorry to have both witnessed the drama and for the person harmed. We can all do better by listening and learning before we speak or take action.

I started 100 Days to Offload challenge today.

These first few days will sort of be figuring out how I want to do this on 100daysof.blog. I’ve started taking daily notes in the MB Notes feature. This may end up being a daily log. This inevitably led me to need to tweak some things. 2024-05-29

I went to the garden center yesterday.

May 29, 2024

After clearing out the pansies and violas that had been in my patio container since last October, I needed summer annuals and a few other garden products. I ended up spending $200 which hit me hard at the time. First, I’m unemployed and chronically ill and every dollar I spend needs to be considered. Second, it seemed like a lot.

It got me thinking about many of the YouTube gardening channels I frequent and how many viewers respond or comment about the amount of money the channel owners spend on plants and infrastructure. I did a little math this morning and the plants themselves cost $130 of the $200, thirty-two plants at roughly $4 a piece (very simplified math). Many of the YouTubers respond to these comments on growing your own annuals from seed to save money, but this is also simplified math.

First of all, most people are already short on time. Accumulating the tools and infrastructure for growing things from seed is also an up-front cost that only pays off if you continue to do it every year. It also isn’t really valuable in terms of time spent if you’re only growing three dozen plants. Another way to save would be to propagate some of the plants at the end of the season and overwinter them indoors. Things like begonia and coleus propagate easily and can be quite happy house plants. But that also takes time and house plants can suffer from pests and disease.

As someone with an energy-limiting chronic illness, my time and energy is just as valuable to me as my money. If I were to view this as a trade-off between what I could charge as an hourly rate as a web developer, I’d only be able to spend about an hour to an hour and a half caring for seedlings or propagations over the winter months to see the benefit. That is frankly unrealistic. I’ve come out of this endeavor now feeling like the money was well spent. There’s no shame in buying your annuals if the cost-benefit to grow your own does not work in your favor.

I’ll be planting the patio containers up this afternoon or tomorrow, so of course I’ll share some pictures when I’m done!

May 25, 2024

The perfect quote to follow up yesterday’s post:

But it is tiring, is it not? The necessity imposed on us to remain cavalier about the one thing in life that holds genuine meaning. Do you not find it lonely?

‚Äî Colin Bridgerton, Bridgerton, S3:E4

I find toxic masculinity and the patriarchy the saddest of conditions for men the most. It holds them hostage to be bound up in system that prevents everyone from finding true happiness in what it means to be human with other humans.

Man or Bear: one woman's perspective

May 24, 2024

What I find the most striking in this piece is her description of sizing up the risk with a million calculations on the spot. This is what many of us have to do to survive under patriarchy on a daily basis, but hearing it described by someone alone in the backcountry makes me really sad.

It could be something he says. Maybe he makes a comment about my body or my appearance. Or he asks if I‚Äôm carrying a weapon and then presses for details about where I‚Äôm camping that night. Sometimes, it‚Äôs a shift in his tone, a leer, the way he puts his body in my space. But, usually, it‚Äôs a combination of things, a totality of behaviors that add up to a singular reality: this man is either not aware that he‚Äôs making me uncomfortable, or he doesn‚Äôt care. Either way, this is the danger zone. Even if he has no intention of harming me, the outcome of that intention is no longer possible for me to assess or predict.

In this moment, my mind snaps into a single, crystalline point of focus. My intuition rises to the surface of my skin. I become a creature of exquisite perception. The world is a matrix of emotional data: visceral, clear, direct.

I need to get away from the man. But I need to do it in a way that doesn‚Äôt anger him. This is the tricky bit. Men who lack social awareness or empathy often also lack other skills in emotional management. And usually, what men in these situations actually want is closeness. They‚Äôre trying to get closer to me, physically or emotionally, in the only way they know how. That combination of poor emotional skillsets and a desire to get closer is exactly what puts me in danger.

The Sick Times: Ten new books about Long Covid, chronic illness, and disability

May 21, 2024

I’ll be looking these up on Libby for Multnomah County Library, but expect: a) a long wait, or b) no availability. I’d love to see a disabled/chronic illness book sharing program, where you could become a member to share costs for physical copies and then ship them back and forth based on a calendar. It probably wouldn’t scale well, but might be useful for a small group of readers.

  1. Pillow Writers Anthology 1: Near-Life Experiences (self-published, 2024)
  2. Illness Politics and Hashtag Activism, By Lisa Diedrich (University of Minnesota Press, 2024)
  3. Moving Mountains: Writing Nature through Illness and Disability, Edited by Louise Kenward (Footnote Press, 2023)
  4. In it for the Long Haul: A Long Covid Journey, by Jon Douglas (self-published, 2024)
  5. Disability Intimacy: Essays on Love, Care, and Desire, by Alice Wong (Vintage Books, 2024)
  6. The Long Covid Reader, Edited by Mary Ladd (Long Hauler Publishing, 2023)
  7. Tilt, by Morgan Stephens (Aldrich Press, 2024)
  8. Move That Cloud, by Dylan‚Äôs Dad (Grow By Reading LLC, 2024)
  9. Do ‚ÄúSomething‚Äù: My Attempt at Nutritional Therapy to Minimize Long-Covid Symptoms (aka the Only Place I‚Äôm Running is Out of Hope), by Vicky Gilpin (Invictus Phoenix Publishing, 2024)
  10. Viral Underclass: The Human Toll When Inequality and Disease Collide, By Steven W. Thrasher (Celadon, 2022)

Officer: “It better be, considering how they chased you out of that co-op.”

Elsbeth: “Oh, yeah, thanks for saving me. Why were they so mad?”

Officer: “You have to be a member to shop there.”

Elsbeth: “Oh, well, that seems exclusive.”

Officer: “Mm. Well, Park Slope is bougie like that.”

Elsbeth: “I thought Brooklyn was supposed to be edgy and grimy.”

Officer: “Well, back in the day, people couldn’t afford Manhattan, so they moved to Brooklyn, but now they can’t afford Brooklyn, either.”

Elsbeth: “Oh, no. So, where do they go?”

Officer: “To their parents.”

üîÜ Little bright spot

May 12, 2024

Good morning from the sun and one of my favorite views in the backyard: the inside curve of the berm.

We are in for another fairly warm day, though hopefully cooler than the last two (86¬∞-90¬∞F depending on your location in the metro). Though I am enjoying the increased sun on this area in the morning with the neighbor’s tree gone, it is a woodland/shaded part of the garden falling under the magnolia tree and I far prefer the dappled shade and shadows during the rest of the day.

Plants in this view, from bottom to top:

  • part of ‘Seaview Sunset’ rhododendron, no longer blooming
  • a dwarf false solomon’s seal ground cover
  • a dwarf hosta
  • a dwarf fern
  • Pacific bleeding heart
  • a hellebore, no longer blooming
  • three heucheras, a dark red-purple leaved one, a light peach-red leaved one, and a dwarf green-leaved one
  • a dianthus, not yet blooming
  • a dwarf geranium, not blooming, barely visible in this picture
  • a sword fern
  • the magnolia tree trunk
  • three azaleas, (mostly) no longer blooming
  • the bottom of a weeping white spruce
  • ‘Czechmark Trilogy’ weigela in the back corner, blooming

2024-05-07

I've had this thought but never put it into words.

May 6, 2024

The modern tech company or capitalistic enterprise is taking cues from fascism and dictators in how they introduce a small piece of controversy, like a testing of the waters. Wait for an “overreaction” by the masses/their audience/core users, wait for that to burnout and normalize, before flooding the zone with too much to fight or argue with all at once. This is the same way fascism causes burnout and complacency and feelings of helplessness, like there’s nothing to be done to fight the thing.

This is what is being done with AI.

Garden Status (2024/05/06)

May 6, 2024

This garden status may be a little premature, but I figure I’d better get it in before the upcoming “heatwave.” We had a very pleasant April, but it was fairly dry, though not overly warm, just…pleasant. This may seem great but it meant that our normally wet spring hasn’t really been so wet. Over the last week, the rain has returned and many of the “May flowers” are sitting with buds-in-waiting for some sunshine so they can do their thing.

It is currently Monday, May 6, 2024, and our forecast high in Portland is to be 57¬∞F. We’ve had gray skies, rain, sun breaks, downpours, hail, and blue skies and sun, all just today. By Friday that high temperature will be 88¬∞F and many things in the garden, having just been doused with two inches of rain, will explode with the sudden temperature increase. Many other things that have had a prolonged and beautiful bloom this spring will begin to fade.

I just made a round in the garden and took some photos. Some things are not quite ready for prime time, I expect I will need to make an interim “garden status” post in a week or two to include some of the plants that really would like to be blooming already but are not.

In the meantime, here are some things I found lovely today and in the past week.

Even disability professionals get it wrong.

May 2, 2024

From the bookmarked article below, a quote that sums up so much of what society gets wrong about disability. To me, I think it is a fundamental misapplication of the prefix “dis-” which means “opposite of.” But in words like “disfunction” the application is not “does not function” it is that it isn’t functioning as expected or as desired. In my opinion, this should be the application of “dis-” in disability as well. Sometimes that simply means that the conditions are not met to create ability through no personal fault, often due to environmental or societal factors knowingly or unknowingly not creating the conditions for ability.

It was very common for disability professionals to frame disability as related to either inability or ability ‚Äì focusing on what people cannot or can do. Many of these professionals thought that disabled people were discriminated against because of their inability to achieve things. This is problematic because it assumes that disabled people are lower in ability. It also assumes disability is all about if a person can achieve something. This is not only not true, it also individualizes disability, blaming the person for their situation, ignoring the many barriers that are placed in people‚Äôs ways.

2024-05-01

It never stops.

Apr 30, 2024

My teenage years were pretty traumatic between finding out about having this terrible illness, being psychologized by doctors, and abandoned by friends and family, and treated terribly by my schools. And that was more than 30 years ago.

This was a story that found me. In 2021, after writing about long Covid, I was accused by the psychiatrist Prof Michael Sharpe of spreading it. Apparently, you could induce such illnesses by discussing them. Investigating further, I was astonished by the failure in his presentation to support his claim with evidence, and perturbed by his lack of satisfactory answers to my questions. Sharpe takes a similarly ‚Äúbiopsychosocial‚Äù approach to ME/CFS, one which at the time of his long Covid presentation still dominated medical practice in the UK.

You can trace the origins of this model to a paper published in 1970. Without assessing a single patient or interviewing a single doctor, it blamed an earlier outbreak of post-viral ME/CFS on ‚Äúmass hysteria‚Äù based on case notes alone. The reasoning included the fact that the outbreak affected more women than men. For centuries, doctors have been readier to classify women‚Äôs illnesses as ‚Äúhysterical‚Äù or psychosomatic than they have men‚Äôs. ME/CFS, like long Covid, hits women harder, so, the thinking goes, it must be all in the mind.

We now know that patients were right to resist interventions that have proved to be both useless and harmful. The impacts were often horrific. A study in Switzerland found that the most powerful factor contributing to suicidal thoughts among people with ME/CFS was ‚Äúbeing told the disease was only psychosomatic‚Äù.

Some patients were forced into these treatment regimes, even locked in psychiatric units to make them comply. Some parents of children with ME/CFS were referred to social services for supposedly encouraging their belief that they were ill. Though unevidenced, the biopsychosocial model influenced the government‚Äôs social security policy, reinforcing its coercive treatment of people seeking disability benefits.

Apr 30, 2024

I am feeling overwhelmed and unprepared and I’m just going to lean into it, because when this happens I tend to want to just shut down and let things happen as they may.

üîÜ Little bright spot

Apr 27, 2024

Today’s little bright spot is complicated.

Yesterday morning, before I’d even made coffee or gotten out of my robe, I heard cracking. The sound of wood doing something it shouldn’t. At first I looked outside in the direction I thought it was coming from and didn’t see anything unusual. Or so I thought. We had a bunch of rain the day before and my neighbor’s apricot tree, freshly laden with leaves, did appear to be hanging a bit low in front of the garage, but not too low.

But the cracking continued. And the tree was falling. All day. Lower and lower. I went outside to try to assess what was going to happen with it, since the direct path of the fall was my garage. I had a pile of mulch in front of the garage, leftover from moving it around my yard, but directly in the way of getting the car out of the garage.

So, there in my robe, having had no coffee, I shoved half the mulch pile into the wheelbarrow and over to the side of the driveway, enough so that I could get the car out of the garage, a) before something terrible happened, or b) the tree branches blocked me from doing so. I managed to do both things still in my robe.

After that I concentrated on calling people: my neighbor, who I had not heard from and who I don’t think was home, my homeowners insurance, unsure if I’d need to make a claim, and several other neighbors to talk about the circumstances. The circumstances as they were that that tree should have come out ten years ago. I’ve hated it since I bought this house. And apparently the neighbor knew it needed to come out and had had the tree service over a week ago to start the removal process.

In Portland if you want to do anything with a tree, you need a permit. Unless you are planting one on private property. But if you are removing one, you need a permit. If you are planting one in a right-of-way like the parking strip, you need a permit. It’s a system that has lofty goals but rarely works to achieve those goals and most people hate the system. First of all, it is slow. Painfully slow.

So while the neighbor was waiting on a permit the tree went ahead and fell on my garage. Now, I don’t know that they knew it was in danger of falling, but I do know that three days ago I was clearing the gutter on that side of the garage in preparation for the rain on Thursday, and I kept hitting my head on a branch that didn’t used to be there so I suspect my neighbor knew something was happening and that the tree was already leaning. I did not recall this hitting-of-my-head until this morning when I was talking with the arborist.

But let’s talk a little bit more about this tree. It is an apricot tree, and a very large old apricot tree, most likely planted back when this neighborhood used be full of empty lots and many vegetable gardens and fruit trees. My yard likely had a fruit tree too. And that house was built in the 50s the same time my garage was built, so it is likely that that apricot tree was in an empty lot. My house was built almost 100 years ago.

Apricot trees, like many stone fruit trees, like warm dry weather, they are not suited to Portland’s climate for the most part. And that tree was never happy. Every spring it would barely bloom as we’d get a late cold snap, and then it would try to leaf out only to be hit with a long wet spring and sporadically drop leaves, sprout more leaves, and drop leaves again. It would try to fruit, but it never succeeded, instead it dropped sappy orange goo onto my garage roof which would then dry and stick to the shingle’s texture and rip it off. Then to top it all off, every fall it would drop all its leaves nearly at once, covering the entire east side of my garage roof, gutter, and the narrow space between the garage and neighbor’s fence. So many leaves, so stinky, that it could fill my green compost bin three or four times.

I hate(d) that tree.

The neighbor was going to remove that tree. They had started the process of doing that and were planning on footing the bill. But as soon as the tree fell and endangered my property, I became responsible for part of it. This is bullshit. I looked high and low for the reasoning that I could tell them they needed to pay for the entire removal without luck. It is viewed as “storm damage” and if it hadn’t damaged my garage would not even be covered by my insurance. Even with my insurance coverage I’d still have to pay my $1000 deductible. I didn’t know if the cost of all the removal + damage would even exceed that.

Friday proved fruitless (heh üòè) in getting an arborist to come out, but my neighbor was able to get the arborist that was going to remove the tree and that was waiting on the city for a permit to come out today, Saturday. In the meantime the tree dug in, so to speak, piercing my garage roof, denting my gutter, and falling lower and lower in front of the garage door. I was under the impression that my garage was holding the entire weight of that tree for 24 hours.

The arborist was very kind. He was actually the owner of a very popular tree service in Portland and did all the removal of the portion on my side himself, from the top of the roof. Another crew member hauled the branches away down my driveway. I ended up paying for the hourly service of two crew members, quoted $600-700 though I have yet to be invoiced. I imagine the bulk of the tree and trunk removal to be quite a bit more than that on my neighbors side, but again, it is their tree and they were planning on removing it anyway. I am bit perturbed by this fact and that they didn’t offer to cover the entire cost. It is frankly a bitch move and not very neighborly.

We ended up being very lucky. The damage to the garage is minimal in the end: two roof shingles and a dented gutter; my neighbor’s fence has some snapped off slats but it was in need of repair anyway. Two things were working in our favor: on my neighbor’s side a 2x4 piece of lumber in part of their kid’s play set was holding up part of the tree’s weight; on my side, a 4x4 concrete-filled steel post used by a gate (overkill much?) was holding up another part of the tree’s weight. It could have been far worse. But I’m still under the deductible so I’m footing all of this work and damage.

So, you are asking, how is this a little bright spot, Anne? I’m doing a lot of complaining.

Well, what if I told you someone came to me months ago and said: for roughly $600 we will remove that tree that you hate so much? You will never have to clean its mess up again and an abundance of light would flood into your backyard making you able to possibly grow vegetables again! Yes! I would absolutely do that! I would pay $600 to never deal with that tree again!

Some Day Notes (2024/04/24)

Apr 24, 2024

It is high time for another note! I’ve made it under a month this time, such an accomplishment. I particularly feel like I need to get this done now, before April ends and May begins, because I will be busy in May, and I’ll explain a bit about that at the end of this note.

The last three weeks have seen me in the garden a lot. I’ve had tasks to complete and the rest of the mulch to spread and I’m back on a weekly mowing schedule, so it is definitely The Season again. I’ve also been busy with more web projects and tinkering and am about to jump into another one in May, though a couple of them are just sitting on the back burner for now while I am occupied by other things.

So let’s get into it!

  • On the same day I posted my last note, I published What I’ve learned about CSS color-scheme and friends. and was surprised and delighted by the positive reactions to it. Nothing is a better cure for imposter syndrome than writing something slightly technical and getting good feedback from a mostly technical audience. I really love this “format” of writing: diving into a topic, making a tangible example/use case, and blogging about it‚ÄîI would do nothing but this if I could!
  • I am continuing the April photo blogging challenge on Micro.blog and doing better this time with not beating myself up over diving into my photo library for many of the pictures. I’ve taken some new ones, but 80% of the entries are probably from the archive.

But we can’t get through life without being judged so I pulled off the bandage and bared my wounds! Really, it turned out pretty great, and I’ve gotten lovely feedback on it as well. Thanks to all those who have told me they liked it! I think my takeaway from the experience and feedback is that there truly is no right way to get your content onto the web and that is really part of the beauty of it üíú
  • 100th weblog post! Fittingly, the Weblog Publishing Workflow post was my 100th weblog post. Adding link previews to echoes on my microblog.

I’m starting with themes.lol using color-mix() with the accent-color used on some form controls with system colors to get a “hue” in both light and dark modes. I’m still working on this, and want to implement it elsewhere, but will definitely have a post on it at some point, likely during the upcoming blogging challenge.

Excuse me while I beleaguer a point.

Apr 23, 2024

We are entering JS Naked Day! We just got through CSS Naked Day a week or so ago! These are great days, and I think the point of them might be getting lost…or at least the point I always got from them: here’s how a site looks, works, and breaks without some of the tools of the web.

It is not only a way to show how well your site works without these tools, but as a cautious tale of how your site breaks without these tools and to provide a lesson perhaps to yourself and others! I want to see these things and I want them to provide guidance on what to do better.

The scramble to perfect the flaws in the days leading up to the so-called Naked Days I think masks the lessons we should be learning. What if instead we bared these imperfections and wrote about it? Maybe put up a caveat on your home page saying “look, I can do better, maybe you can too” because this might be one day for you and me, but some people for one reason or another experience the web every day without these tools.

I hope I don’t sound like I’m preaching from an ivory tower, as I did not participate in either this year, though I do plan to in the future. I absolutely appreciate those who do, even when they scramble to fix bugs. But I’d also love to see what we’ve taken for granted and how we can work to make the web better through these little revelatory exposures.
