Grandiloquent Notes (2023/12)

Dec 23, 2023

It has been nearly a month since I wrote a Some Day Note! In realizing this a week ago I fretted a bit but then by putting it off another week I now can just use this as a reason to write a year-end note. But of course, I had to come up with a name because if there’s one thing I like to do, it is to name things.

Grandiloquent Notes is born!

I was trying to generate a name that somewhat poked fun at year-end traditions. I’ve always disliked resolutions. The idea of looking back at an entire year and prophesying always felt bombastic. I was actually trending towards some rather morbid terms but then decided against that.

I landed on “grandiloquent” by rabbit-holing on Merriam-Webster from “note” to “resonant” to “resonance” to “grandiloquence” to “grandiloquent,” which ultimately is “grand speech” from Latin loqui (to speak) and grandis (grand or great). I think this is just about perfect: pokes fun at the thing but at the same time gives me permission to go with it. My favorite type of naming.

Let’s dive in!

As with the previous three years, 2023 has been a tough one. The world just wants to move on from something that refuses to move on from them. Denials on the continued spread of a disease that is killing and disabling thousands is on all of us and should sit with you during this season. Many people will stay home alone due to conditions they already have that prevent them from even taking a chance of catching it lest it make them worse, or kill them. Many more will gather with supposed loved ones and spread that disease, knowingly or unknowingly. I don’t know how we tackle some of our existential societal challenges if we can’t even agree on this. That’s all I’m going to say on it. This is one of the hardest times in my life and I’ve lost so much faith in my fellow humans.

On a much more positive note, though I will not go as extensively into it as I will in an upcoming weblog post, I sort of fell into joining a few online “communities” this year. I spent 18 months in 2018 and 2019 in the worst stages of burnout at a job I’d ever encountered. It broke me so badly, and then was followed up by a global pandemic and waves of intense hiring and firing, that I had basically vowed to never work in the industry again. I went years without writing a single line of code. I fell into omg.lol in late January 2023 after: a) coming across the social.lol Mastodon instance and wanting to join it, and b) hearing the Do By Friday episode where they talked about it.

I then also signed up for Micro.blog, but forgot about it (I have talked about this elsewhere before) until the summer when I finally subscribed and got moving on incorporating a microblog into the rest of my web life. Over the next 11 months I’ve pretty much changed my whole feeling on the web and tech industry. I’ve jumped back in with some reservations and boundaries that I will attempt to hold onto lest I fall into the same traps that burned me before. I am concentrating on the small, intentional, and independent web and purposely avoiding the capitalist web. If I hope to ever work on the web again, it won’t be for but rather with. An important distinction. I will not let capitalism consume my remaining years.

This year has also seen some hard times in my family. I’m getting up there in age, but my parents are really getting up there in age. My father is now the primary caretaker of house and home, where it was always my mother before her recent health issues. He’s having to learn how to do everything that he never had to do before. She’s cognitively declining but also fighting a body that is slowly deteriorating beneath her. Eventually I will need to be closer to them as my father is going to start to fail at some point too. I imagine 2024 to be tumultuous in many ways.

I participated in two monthly photography challenges on Micro.blog, one in March before I was on Micro.blog (even though I already had an account! silly me…) and another in September. I enjoyed these but rather also enjoy my personal habit of posting a (nearly) daily featured photo from the iPadOS Photos app. I started writing semi-regular “notes” called Some Day Notes (reference above, tied to this note), also on my microblog. And I’m slowly dipping into creating a custom theme for my microblog, currently I’m using Tiny Theme with a thick layer of CSS customization (and a few templates).

I challenged myself in November to participate in National Blog Posting Month and to write something every day of the month, and I succeeded! I really never thought I would do it, but I did! It was quite an experience. I ended the month having written over 25,000 words and writing 30 blog posts split (not evenly) between weblog and my microblog. I’ve had some incredible traffic on weblog and some quite popular posts. One post was featured in a popular front-end curated newsletter (Friday Front-end). Another made it into a popular web-related podcast (ShopTalk Show). And still another (just last week!) was in Jeremy Keith’s (AKA Adactio) link list üò± I have been exposed to incredible people in this process and feel less alone than at any point during this pandemic, but also on the web, even when I was working in-person with people in an office.

Weblog has been my home this year. I’ve really poured quite a bit into it and it serves as my hub on the web, where all other parts of me collide. I first cobbled together a custom theme for myself in order to figure out how weblog worked and what could be accomplished. It’s really how I learn best, by doing and tinkering. And now my whole web world lives on a custom domain: anniegreens.lol. I’ve put so much into it I’m now on a side quest to create styles and custom patterns for weblog themes. I acquired three new omg.lol addresses: styles, custom, and themes (the last one gifted to me by omg.lol) as well as another domain: themes.lol. I will be working on this in the coming months.

I think the above covers my “big ticket items” for the year. Absolutely nothing exciting, but then again, I haven’t had Covid one single time and I’m healthier than I have been in a couple years due to concentrating on keeping myself exposure-free, eating better, listening to my body’s signals, and reducing stress, crashes, and PEM (ME/CFS hallmark condition). I also haven’t had a secondary infection of any kind this entire pandemic because I wear a mask everywhere in public. Prior to the pandemic, and due to my existing chronic illness, I would get sick several times a year with colds, sinus infections, the flu, you name it. Nothing since I’ve stopped breathing other people’s expired air. Amazing what we could accomplish if everyone would get over their selfish “rights” (to get sick and get other people sick).

The only other part to this will be listing out and linking to all my Some Day Notes for the year, only starting in August, which include other specifics and highlights. In case you missed them at the time or want to look back, or you can ignore them and this post altogether!

Happy Holidays and a Merry New Year to you and yours‚Äîstay safe out there!

Dec 15, 2023

This is what I have been thinking their entire purpose is.

A story and quote close to home.

Dec 15, 2023

As I have mentioned before, I have ME/CFS, and have since childhood/adolescence. My mother and I spent years traveling around to doctors and specialists in the late 80s/early 90s, only to finally be given an “answer” but then be further psychologized and traumatized in the years following, so much that I just simply stopped talking about it or seeking help. I had to find ways within myself to function and they forced me to redefine what life could and would look like at such a vulnerable young age.

Many reading this will carry similar stories about themselves, often without truly realising it. In my case they came from medical professionals ‚Äì doctors, nurses, physiotherapists, psychotherapists ‚Äì but for other people the list will include family and friends. Even the most well-meaning of onlookers can unintentionally promote the idea of willpower as a means of overcoming illness. And for all of us, there is the additional influence of society and the media, with their emphasis on the sick and disabled as a drain on resources. It can be hard to escape the message that we must try harder. Do better. Make an effort to be less demanding.

I look forward to reading more about Naomi’s journey of discovery, as I too am on a similar journey, though different and probably for contrasting reasons. Naomi seems to be doing better in her illness; I am doing worse.

Yay üôÑ. It’s a popular recommendation to “leave the leaves” but when you live in a mild and wet winter climate this can lead to harboring fungal and insect issues in your garden. So, I do leave some leaves, but I really cannot leave a foot’s worth of thick magnolia and apricot leaves stacked on my ornamental garden beds unless the leaves are mulched first. Suffice to say, I’ve spent hours to days raking leaves and cleaning gutters over the past few weeks.

May 28, 2011

A level at which one functions with ease and familiarity.

Nov 25, 2023

I’m traveling to visit family today so I won’t have time to do a long thought-provoking or detailed post for NaBloPoMo. What I do want to explore, though, is an experience, or set of experiences, from yesterday.

Yesterday I did a bunch of stuff: baked some muffins, watched some TV, did some yard work, housekeeping, etc. And I worked on a seventh part to a series on microformats I’ve published on weblog. As with all NaBloPoMo posts I’ve been keeping tally on micro.blog and linking to the weblog posts.

From the morning until I went to bed I was unable to get any logged-in pages on micro.blog to load for me. They would all time out. So, I was unable to post my tally, though I did complete my weblog post. This morning I killed the session(s) and some cache and tried again and finally got in.

Also, last night, after publishing my weblog post, I discovered some URLs on weblog weren’t getting special characters from the title stripped out. This seemed to start around Wednesday this week, but I had only noticed it yesterday. It was only affecting two posts to begin with. But once I edited a previous post to add a link to the new post in the series, that post too was affected. Something in configuration was probably changed that I didn’t have control over. I’m sure it will get resolved, but in the meantime I needed to make some changes of my own so that readers weren’t hitting 404s.

I added a few “URL map” entries to weblog config, so that anyone hitting the old URLs would get the posts. However, without remembering this happens, after saving the config, ALL weblog posts had their URLs updated, and any special characters in their titles were then in the URLs üò±. This was not good.

Luckily, I can manually add “Location” metadata for preferred paths to weblog posts, but I’ve got a lot of posts! So that’s how I spent my evening. I was fortunate to have had an Archive page open that I DID NOT REFRESH with the correct URLs to reference and diligently added a location path for every post. It was not a great night, but my weblog is not a spring chicken anymore, and there are some posts that get quite a bit of traffic. And it being NaBloPoMo, the blog is just getting hit more. I did not want a day that I was going to be away (today) to be full of people hitting 404s.

Everything seems stable now, and hopefully whatever config was causing the issue will get resolved. But between that and having the issue loading micro.blog I realized how attached I’ve become to my blogs! I just had no idea how ten months of blogging has made me so reliant on that warm blanket. Yes, it’s a comfort zone that I now rely on.

Just an interesting thing to consider.

NaBloPoMo: 25/30

POSSE copies:

Nov 25, 2023

Entry 24/30 for National Blog Posting Month (NaBloPoMo) is on weblog.

Diving into indiewebify.me & microformats, Part VII

Microformats properties for citations using a previous weblog post as an example.

Note: the weblog post was published 11/24/2023 but I couldn’t get micro.blog to load to post.

Being thankful.

Nov 23, 2023

It might be clich√© but I figured I’d give this sort of post a shot this year. These are thoughts I’ve had before but never written them down (or typed them, as be the case). Seems like the right kind of day, Thanksgiving, to document them.

I just wrapped up a three-part series on a subject that has been inside me and growing for the last five or six years. I consider it a first draft even though it is published on weblog. I do expect to revisit it and refine it over time. It was tough to write. It was sitting in Drafts since March or April and I just couldn’t get a move-on finishing it.

It has been a tough few years, I think for everyone the world over. Sometimes I think people have a hard time sitting with their grief and really letting it take ahold of them. But I think that is an important process and it never goes as planned nor takes the time we expect it should. I consider the last five to six year of my life to be one rolling grief process, not all about one thing, and not all at the same time. It has felt like just as I was rounding the corner something else was there to surprise me again.

Through it all I’ve been immensely grateful for my mother and father. At the beginning of the pandemic, for the first year, as I was still reeling with burnout from my job, I was only concerned about their well-being and keeping them alive until vaccines became available. During the second year I lost an uncle to Covid. My parents are still alive, though my mother’s health is declining, due to factors unknown, though I have suspicions. However, I’m thankful to still have a good relationship with them and be within driving distance.

I’m thankful I still have my home. Through this time I’m not sure how I would have gotten by with my illness if I had to share space with anyone else who wasn’t as careful as I need them to be. Not only as careful, but as honest as I would need them to be. People don’t want to always share their carelessness or mistakes and that honesty is life or death to immunocompromised people.

I’m also grateful my home has the space for a garden. I have painted this garden like a canvas, every single plant I put in myself (or via the gardener I used to have help me when I was still working full-time and had less expendable time to spend working in the yard). Now I do it all myself, all maintenance, except for some of the trees I can’t get to, which is really only the street trees and the neighbor’s apricot tree that hangs over my garage.

The garden has also been the thing that pulled me through burnout, the continuing pandemic, health issues, struggles with my aging parents, tribulations with city living, and a whole host of other things. Escaping into my garden is a joy that is irreplaceable, a salve that can calm even the worst of days.

Even though the climate is changing fast, I’m thankful for the Pacific Northwest’s still mostly mild temperate climate, its immense beauty, and inviting outdoor activities. I’m thankful and remorseful for those who came before us, who ceded land that they never believed they owned, for promises white man has never kept.

I’m glad I’m mostly surrounded by open-minded liberals and a neighborhood of people that I get along with and look out for each other. And even though this country may be on the verge of something terrible if they don’t make the right choice next year, I’m thankful we can still mostly be called a democracy.

Finally, I am thankful I have found some joy in online activity after the past few years. That I still have hope that I could work in some capacity in tech or web development again, and that blogging has brought about a type of release, a space to find my voice and place on the web again. Thank you to both omg.lol and micro.blog and the Fediverse.

I hope you all have lovely and safe holidays with those you choose to surround yourselves with ü¶Éüíúüôè

Just a little note about the power of blogging.

Nov 20, 2023

One of my most “popular” posts on weblog (based on hit count) continues to be On micro.blog and how I want to use it.. I’m still not entirely sure how it continues to make the rounds, but it does. I also haven’t even started to make a dent in all my wishes and dreams put forth in that post. I continue to plod away on todos on weblog, mostly.

I recently added the “Reply by email” plug-in on my microblog and setup email via my domain using Fastmail. I then proceeded to hesitatingly add a “Reply by email” link to the end of every weblog post and asked Adam to supply a UUID/unique variable available at the template level to set wildcard per-post email addresses to try to harness spam and source if or when it started to occur.

I got my very first email from weblog this morning and it was from that post! And it wasn’t spam or harassment. In fact, it was thanking me for the post and saying that they had just signed up for Micro.blog because of it. How great is that?

Signs of the season.

Nov 15, 2023

Since around 2018 I’ve put my Christmas tree up the same weekend the US turns its clocks back to Standard Time. That is usually around the first week in November and it heralds a dark time to come. This ritual is to ward off the dark time, it provides a warm glow in the corner of my “great room”‚Äînot actually a great room, but the living room and dining room that is only separated by a large opening as is common in Craftsman houses‚Äîthat I sorely need during the months ahead.

In Western Oregon and Washington, the dark months are dark due to shortened day length but also due to a brooding sky prone to drop water on us at any moment. Low hanging clouds can make even the middle of the day seem dark even if water isn’t coming out of them. A clock that prematurely brings the day to an “end” at 4:30pm is almost unbearable. So I augment the day with false warmth and fake light.

This year I missed that weekend by nine days, but I got it up yesterday nonetheless. For the remainder of November I’ll slowly bring up decorations and adornments and take my time putting trinkets and ribbon and garland all over the tree. I’m not a giant Christmas fan, but something about this slow moving tradition, that I often spend by myself, is cathartic.

I didn’t have a Christmas tree at all until I bought this house twelve years ago. So all the way from my mid-20s until my mid-30s I went without, except when visiting my parents. I lived in apartments, one of which was in a Pearl District tower surrounded by other large buildings, and faced north. That was a dark time indeed. For seven years I lived in that lovely cave and my well-being suffered from it. It faced the Weiden+Kennedy building and I remember one year they started putting a lights-only Christmas tree on the roof-top deck. That was my Christmas tree.

So now this slowly decorated tree will sit in a corner through November, December, and January, and won’t come down until February, sometimes all the way until Valentine’s Day. It has helped so much. I’m even considering adding some exterior lights this year. I have a pre-lit garland for the front porch, but I’m thinking of something different that I can see from my upstairs. My garage has a window facing out to the back yard and there is a plain unadorned grapevine wreath hanging on the inside of it. I want to string it up with lights and put it on a timer. The only people that will see it will be me and my immediate neighbors via their back yards. I sort of love that idea. Like a little secret circle of warmth.

Nov 12, 2023

America’s a terribly difficult idea filled with promise and impossible to live up to. Promise is inchoate and promise is what binds us. Some of us died, some got sick, some got rich, some…had bad luck, some of us were fortunate, more than others.

But failed promise only truly fails when it leads to lowered expectations.

‚ÄîCJ, The West Wing on MAX, Season 4, Episode 13: The Long Goodbye

I always joke about living in an urban jungle.

Nov 11, 2023

If I wanted to get serious about getting Backyard Bird Habitat certified, I probably could. I have all the sizes and types of birds. Big birds, little birds, tiny birds, hummingbirds. On any given day I see a half dozen to a dozen different species of bird in and around my yard in an inner city neighborhood about three miles from downtown, about one mile from a major river and Interstate highway.

Today I was cleaning up some leaves. I intended to only do a little bit of work, but it stopped raining and was fairly pleasant, and being a holiday weekend, the neighborhood was a ghost town. So I ended up staying out there for 3.5 hours and cleared leaves off the front sidewalk and storm drain, the driveway, garage and patio gutters, patio (which had the most bizarre shaped accumulations of leaves blown in by wind this morning), and the area under the neighbor’s apricot tree between the garage and fence. Here, I encountered a surprise.

I had been raking over there for a good ten minutes or so. I’d formed several good sized piles, but something made me look up. I do recall seeing feather fuzz all about and thinking it was an odd time for molting. The fuzz was someone’s brunch. I looked up into the apricot tree and not six feet away was a Cooper’s Hawk staring back at me, fresh catch in talons, feathers all over the large moss covered branch, and little bit of something on its beak.

I was completely taken aback. I’ve seen a Cooper’s Hawk twice around my yard. In fact, I just had a short conversation about this on Mastodon a week or so ago. The first encounter I was bent over in my front yard and just as I was standing up straight a Cooper’s Hawk was swooping over the top of me, recognized by its thickly banded tail feathers. The second encounter was in the back yard, I was upright this time, and it came straight out of the group of trees near the garages and torpedoed over the top of me, again very recognizable.

This time the bird was caught off guard too. Like I said, the neighborhood was very quiet today, hardly any humans around. I remembered being upstairs and hearing the crows making a ruckus, they were definitely not happy with something and I first thought it might be a Red-tailed Hawk, which very often get mobbed by the crows from all around when they dare to land in a large tree. I think now it was the Cooper’s, but the hawk still got its meal.

I managed to get some photos, because I don’t think it quite knew what to do about me. I was very close and I don’t think they see well at close distances. We were both in/under the tree so it was pretty dim light and I only had my iPhone, and not a very new one either. I talked to it for a little while and didn’t make many quick moves, but I did go back and forth to my rolling compost bin to empty several buckets of leaves and it remained on the branch the entire time, though frozen and no longer eating its catch. Eventually I went around the back of the garage out of sight and when I came back it was gone.

Day tripping.

Nov 9, 2023

I had some appointments this week and I took a little road trip into Washington. I visited family and went to my childhood home to see my parents. My mom had back surgery last week and has been having a rough recuperation.

Normally, my visits would include a giant walk around their several acre garden, but that wasn’t in the cards for her this time. However, with the outstanding fall color the PNW is experiencing this year, I took the circuitous route around the garden alone, and captured some very pretty and somewhat morose photographs.

Morose because every time I go visit is a reminder that my parents will not be around forever, that I can’t stay in Portland much longer, and that I will end up back at that house with them until they die. It is all so insurmountable and hard to comprehend. It took so much out of me to leave that place. Not just the house, I had already left there. But that town, that county, everything about it is wrapped up in a history that is no longer me and needing to go back makes me feel like part of the me now will die.

I still have no idea what this will look like. I don’t know if I can finish work on my house, drawing on savings and retirement, and rent it until a future time that I could come back. Or if I need to make a clean break and sell it, not knowing what that future will look like, or how hard it would be to manage a back-and-forth for any period of time.

As much as I love the location and immediate surroundings of my parents' house and I wouldn’t mind being there I don’t want to be in that town. It’s a very backwards place with very little to offer and a whole lot of the yucky-ness that is plaguing the US right now. The world, right now. If I break all ties with Portland, there’s no way I can financially come back later. I’d have to keep the house I have now in order to come back later.

Like I said, insurmountable decisions my head just swims with all there is to plan and execute. Please enjoy these photos from the day in the garden.

Nov 2, 2023

Heck yeah! A Haunting in Venice is on Hulu. I will be watching this and it is looking far more horror-like than I normally tolerate. But how do I not watch a modern making of a Poirot? I’ve enjoyed all the other remakes of this vintage with Kenneth Branagh portraying Poirot, so I guess I must.

Dynamicism.

Nov 2, 2023

Today is a much different day than yesterday. Yesterday my body woke up around 4:45am and by 5am I was ready for coffee. There’s no “going back to sleep” and no more excuses, just give me coffee.

While in the kitchen, I decided, as I listened to podcasts and sipped coffee, to prep everything I needed for making my favorite potato soup. That’s a fair bit of chopping and collecting of mise en place. And then, at barely 7am I was already making the soup.

By 8:30am I had a giant pot of soup going into the refrigerator and I was sitting down at my computer absorbing all the things. I launched into deciding to participate in NaBloPoMo and wrote my first entry. I collected my weekly grocery delivery from the porch box, made a giant salad for lunch, and had two slices of pumpkin pie. It was now early afternoon.

You might be thinking “what’s the big deal?” The big deal is, that is unusual activity. I’m not bipolar. I’m not depressed. I don’t knowingly have ADHD or autism, though some parts of my condition do overlap. But I do have another invisible, and quite dynamic, chronic illness. And today I’m paying the price for that gusto of life.

I’ve had ME/CFS for the better part of my 47 years. As a child and teen I was quick and smart, outgoing, and participated in many school and community sports, as well as took long hiking and cycling trips with my father, uncles, and cousins.

In grade school I kicked ass in every year’s athletic competence tests, beating all the boys. I was in advanced courses and made straight A’s in school all the way to my junior year in HS when everything changed. Some time in the years preceding that change I had a virus, probably Mononucleosis (EBV).

This post isn’t necessarily intended to be a history of my illness, though bits of that will naturally come out. Instead, this post is a revelation of my dynamicism and how hard it is to exist in a world that expects so much sameness. Most of the time people won’t see or sometimes hear from me on my worst days. This is less to do with hiding and much more to do with capability. As such, when I am seen or heard from it is usually when I’m most capable. This leads to the expectation that I’m always capable.

But my illness has conditions. Conditions within conditions. It is always a trade-off, everything I do. How much of this can I do without spending the next three days to a week paying for it? What plans do I have that are non-negotiable vs what lovely weather we have today and I want to spend all of it in the garden? Can I do a giant grocery order and use every spoon I’ve saved as well as many spoons I might need into the next couple weeks just so I can feed myself?

The world is hard. I wish people would just believe other people. I have spent many years fighting off gaslighting and disbelief. Being brushed aside as lazy or incompetent. Mostly fueled by the limited exposure many of us with this illness give to the wider world. We spend a lot of time alone. Many of us have been abandoned by family and friends. Even those that stick around don’t always “get it” and trying to educate anyone is one of the hardest things. That is taking spoons I might need to vacuum my house.

I’m sure I’ll write more about this. It won’t be a straight line and will meander, but with the state of a still-continuing pandemic and ever growing cohort of Long Covid sufferers, even those getting an ME/CFS diagnosis, it’s important to talk about this much maligned and ignored condition. Be gracious and understanding with each other. We are dynamic and deserve love and respect.

Oct 31, 2023

At some point someone said to me the hardest time of the week is 11am on a Tuesday because Monday you have the excuse it is Monday, but by Tuesday you realize everything you didn’t get done on Monday, and you are looking forward to the middle of the week the next day with too much still to get done.

Some Day Notes (2023/10/31)

Oct 31, 2023

Time for another Some Day Note! It’s been a longer period than the last few, not sure why that is. Nothing particularly interesting or exciting happened, but let’s see what I have for you today!

  • Something I mentioned doing in the last note, I started a photoblogging series on Ceilings, Chandeliers, and Light Fixtures. I’m on a little break as I resume some Featured Photo posts, but there are more to come.
  • I made a short trip to visit family. My mom is having back surgery today and I wanted to get up there to see them before that time, as I’m not sure how she’ll progress afterwards and through the holidays.
  • Portland had a very early frost/freeze this year. In my zip code the average first frost date is November 22, so this came almost four weeks early. The garden didn’t really flinch and we’re back to mild temps and rain into next week.
  • I talked a bit about the top floor in my house that I’m calling the treehouse lair and how my plans and usage for the space have evolved over a decade living here.
  • The leaf raking season is getting into full swing. I only really clear off hard surfaces (and gutters) and mostly leave them on the garden beds (though not smothering plants). Once the rains really set in and the leaves start to break down, I do clear some of them out of the garden beds in order to avoid harboring diseases that can affect the crowns of some plants in our overly wet winter climate.
  • The birds are back in the yard and I’m so happy to see them. We have juncos year round but they tend to go to higher elevations during the heat of summer. But now I have them and: song sparrows, goldfinches, house finches, chickadees, bushtits…OH MY! The best season for birds.
  • Speaking of birds, I took one of my cars into the DEQ last week and the station I went to is next to a slough on the Columbia River and partially surrounded by a thick tree canopy. While waiting in line next to a single tree in the middle of the paved area next to the waiting lanes I saw a female downy woodpecker! She was only about six feet from my open car window, but of course I didn’t get a picture, she flew away as the car in front of me pulled forward.
  • Yesterday I published a post on weblog about the feature I suggested to Vincent for Tinylytics, and included a hearty thank you to all indie developers.
  • I’m back to working on integrating microformats into weblog after an update from @kizu about doing the same during IndieWebCamp this week. I really got into this in the spring and started documenting what I was learning and doing in a post, but somehow got delayed. Back to it (and a post!)
  • I’m also resuming a personal post on work and the state of the web. I’m prompted to revisit this after several blog posts and a conference talk made the rounds recently. It’s been hard to write but I think finishing it will be cathartic for me and hopefully others.
  • This coming weekend is the clock setback for Daylight Saving Time in the US. As is tradition for me since around 2018, I will be putting up my Christmas tree. This began as a way to ward off the dark sads, not because I’m a huge Christmas fan. I’ll take the month of November to get it decorated, I’m not particularly in a hurry, and then it will stay up until around Valentine’s day next year.

Take care and have a great week!

Today’s featured photo from the Photos app (2023/10/26)

I’ve been waiting patiently for this photo to come up, it remains one of my all-time favorite and best photos. See more about it in a series on my now defunct Substack newsletter: Southern Oregon Coast, a Series.

September 4, 2017

The treehouse lair.

Oct 25, 2023

My upstairs is transitional. When I bought the house, the two rooms were finished: one room had tacky paneling and was basically a very large room for storage with two porthole windows over the front porch; the back room was a nicely finished bedroom with carpet, plaster walls, a nice sized walk-in closet and three large windows facing out over the backyard. But none of it was climate controlled, updated, or ecological.

When I had a whole home energy upgrade in the first couple years of living here, I had them tear it all out. Replaced all the windows with energy efficient windows. Updated all the electrical. Had the old furnace chimney dismantled all the way down three floors to the basement and ran updated HVAC up (there was zero previous heating/cooling on the top floor prior). And had spray foam and rigid foam insulation installed throughout.

So, the area up there is more comfortable than it ever was before, though it is “technically” not finished anymore due to there being no sheetrock or other wall surface. The original wood floor in the “bedroom” part has been revealed under the carpet that was there. The stairwell is tongue and groove wood panelling, which I really like and wouldn’t mind having all the way into the bedroom. The framing for the closet is still in place, and I have all the original doors and hardware still. It really just needs wall covering and window trim.

This is now a priority as in the subsequent years I have started using the space more and more. I first started using it as an office away from my home office on the ground floor. Mostly to “get away”, as it is so quiet up there and it gives a different perspective facing out the back of the house over the trees. Also, as the magnolia tree that I planted in 2013 has become a Real Tree now, it really does feel like a treehouse up there and I quite love it.

Lately, over the course of the pandemic, being home more and more, I started sleeping up there too. Mostly during the winter since I love hearing the rain on the roof. My bedroom in my childhood home was upstairs too and that is a sound I miss hearing at night. I made the bedroom into a “guest room” with a twin bed that I use when I sleep up there and I usually get some of the best sleep when I’m up there.

What I was waiting on was funds to push out a dormer and install a master bath above the main floor bath, before finishing the walls and trim. But that may be unlikely in my current financial situation as well as the length of time I might remain here, which is unknown. It is still likely that could be done, but finishing the space as it is is priority now: walls and trim, paint, light fixtures, finish wood floors. I’m not sure I’ll do anything with the other room than leave it as storage for now.

Since I’ve been sleeping up there, though, I wanted for some privacy, even though it is unlikely there is anyone able to see in. I do look across to my neighbor’s upstairs dormer, but I can’t see into it so I doubt they can see into mine across the distance of our two backyards combined. Still, I decided to rig something up today. I put up some Command hooks and took a clean drop cloth and used four binder clips (I’m still amazed that I come up with new uses for these‚Äîso versatile!) and have a rough privacy curtain but can still see out the top half of the double hung window in the middle when standing. I sort of love it.

The Sellwood Bridge

Oct 18, 2023

Capturing the YouTube videos for the Sellwood Bridge move and construction time-lapses because I feel like I’m looking them up from time-to-time.

This is definitely part of my history of living in Portland. I used to work in John’s Landing off Macadam and spent time in Sellwood often, and watched the progress of the bridge degrade before the county went through this move and replacement process.

It’s also important to understand the history of the Sellwood Bridge and how it was always undersized and poorly constructed was tied to Portland’s very history of wealth and corruption.

  • Rampant political corruption resulted in a weak Sellwood Bridge, The Oregonian/OregonLive
  • Sellwood Bridge History, Multnomah County

Some Day Notes (2023/10/11)

Oct 11, 2023

I’ve given a short update in a recent micro.blog post but I’m due for an updated Some Day Note, so here I am! What do I have for you today?

  • I’m considering a new mini photo series on my microblog. This came about from seeing a post by @mph about his recent stop at Camp 18 and he shared more photos from over the years. This had me searching for my photos from Camp 18 and I eventually used “chandelier” as a search term in Photos and now want to share all my glorious ceilings, chandeliers, and light fixtures, from lots of places, which I have far more of than I expected. More on this to come!
  • I finished my Manual of Me. I’ve been working on this for quite a while, probably more therapy for myself than something for anyone else to gain value from, but who knows. If I ever hope to work again, outside of health limitations that may endure, I need to get back into a headspace that I haven’t been in for some time. I think working through these sections and thinking back to what challenges I had at my last job and what I would hope to get from a new job was an important step.
  • I wrote an Accessibility Statement for weblog. This was something I had been considering adding but was recently prompted to get it done in order to feel right in joining the a11y-webring.club organized by accessibility advocate and developer, Eric Bailey.
  • On that note: I joined the webring and I want to join more! Invite me to your webring!
  • As part of preparing my Accessibility Statement, I ran through an accessibility audit using the WAVE web accessibility evaluation tool provided by WebAIM on all weblog template pages and important landing and one-off pages. Afterwards I made several important CSS and config updates and improvements, some of which had been on my list for several months.
  • I had a “tiny” üòÅ (and related to the previous entries) tinylytics drama again. I discovered a few dozen hits from what I assumed was somebody or something trying to find a vulnerability on weblog via a path that doesn’t exist (and therefore results in a 404): /data/getpage.php. I reported this to Vincent, requesting the hits be removed and suggested some way via the admin to flag further hits for removal. Because Vincent is great and always helpful, he informed me that the origin of the hits was WebAIM‚Äîmy fault all along! He had already removed the hits, but now I know when they show up again where they’re coming from. In the future there will be some admin tool for specifying hit paths to ignore. That’s a great feature that I can see being very useful.
  • I listened to a really great episode of Shop Talk Show interviewing Manton about micro.blog and I’m again on the edge of wanting to upgrade my subscription to premium. Soon.
  • We’re fast approaching mid-October when the real rainy season can be relied on. A welcome turn as the rain is back in Portland this week after another hot spell last week when temps went into the mid-80s again.
  • Despite this temperature hiccup, I am gearing up to do winter containers on the patio. This is always a cathartic ritual of tearing out my summer annuals and putting in pansies and violas that will continue all the way through until spring, bringing color and blooms off-and-on through the dark days. These blooms are not only popular with me, but also “my” hummingbirds, as I’ve discovered them drinking from them often.
  • I’m finally getting close to selling one of my cars. It’s a boring story about why I have two cars, which I obviously do not need as someone not even working full-time right now, and even when I was, I worked from home for the five years prior. While I was filling out some tools online for valuation I inadvertently added the mileage of my other vehicle, but when I tried to correct it was not allowed to due to some other mileage the tool claimed to have knowledge of‚Äîwhere is that mileage coming from? I’m not sure and am bothered by the need to call some entity to correct it. That will have to be dealt with though…üòí I am the only owner of the car so I can’t imagine.
  • I have some Arlo security cameras that need replaced. They are no longer supported by Arlo, though at least Arlo was persuaded from completely bricking them after customer uproar. But they also require batteries and do not tie-into HomeKit, which I am quite fond of nowadays. I settled on some indoor Eve Home cameras. I’ve only had them set up for a few days but am already liking the improved video quality and the automatic mode switching that occurs via Home members coming and going. They also use Apple Secure Video which is stored in iCloud.
  • So glad that the baking and soup season is upon us! I made an ad hoc soup last week to celebrate despite the hot weather and it turned out to be delicious. I would make it again!
  • My weblog post “The picture element for unexpected art direction." made it in Friday Front-End! I am both humbled and horrified.

Another note with quite a few entries, I suppose that’s enough for now‚Äîtake care and have a great week!

Yesterday I wrapped up my longtime coming Manual of Me. Earlier in the week I wrote an Accessibility Statement and joined the a11y-webring.club. I‚Äôve also been working on my list of updates and fixes, both in config and CSS.

I wanted to make soup today.

Oct 6, 2023

Though the weather certainly does not call for it, it is fall and I insist. Soup is probably my favorite food, along with a good casserole or pasta dish or everything deli sandwich or breakfast burrito. There’s a pattern here. I like dishes with a lot in them. Many textures, flavors, hearty. I like my salads the same way. Pasta salads too.

I sorely need to go grocery shopping but thought I could cobble together an “Anything Goes Italian Soup” with what I had on hand. I found about 2/3 cup pearled barley, a couple cans of cannellini beans, a large can of San Marzano whole-peeled tomatoes, an onion, 1/2 head of minced garlic, carrots/broccoli/power greens, red pepper flakes, salt and fresh ground black pepper, and a healthy dose of Italian seasoning.

I think it looks great, we’ll see how it tastes. It should last me a few days, but some of it may end up in the freezer for a rainy day, or a dull day this winter, or just when I’m out of spoons to make anything else.

Edit: it needed a little something, I topped with balsamic vinegar, and that did it üòã

Human-ing is hard.

Oct 4, 2023

Finally finished a weblog post yesterday, after starting and stopping work on it over the past few weeks. It wasn’t long or hard, but it was involved and included call-outs via Mastodon posts from seeking a solution to a technical problem and finding that we can still rely on each other sometimes.

Today I’m trying to write an Accessibility Statement for weblog in order to join another webring. This was prompted by posting that I was going to add a line to my Webrings page seeking more webrings to join. I asked if that would seem desperate, so far the jury is hung on that üòÜ

What do these two things have in common? They involve other humans, and anything involving other humans makes the task that much harder. I wanted to write that weblog post in the most respectful way that I’d be sure to attribute the right people and information (something as of this morning I’m still not entirely confident of!) Writing an accessibility statement is both opening myself up to criticism from other people, but also laying bare my doubts that I even know what I think I know!

Layered on top of the difficulty writing already holds, it is no wonder we can sit on in-progress work for long periods of time and sometimes never publish it. Anyway, I didn’t intend for this little post to be in depth. I just wanted to acknowledge the human-ness we all face even in places we might not have thought to be so human.

Some shots today before the sun came around the side of the house.

Sep 28, 2023

This is the narrow side yard that used to be entirely grass prior to 2021. I had all the grass removed and a basalt stone path put in with river rock running up to a narrow planting bed. I chose the plants and put them in myself in August of 2021, so this is two years old.

Looking in the opposite direction to an area I call “behind the berm” where some shade plants and hydrangeas live. The hydrangeas are entering their best season, in my opinion, when the take on their fall color. Those bloom heads are not the summer flowering color but the more mauve to red they take on before turning nearly burgundy. The leaves will go from green to yellow to orange to red as well. The multi-trunk tree on the right is my magnolia.

I wrote about this area in my newsletter last year if you’re interested:

  • Behind the Berm (on Substack)
  • Behind the Berm (raw import to Buttondown)

üå≤ Day 27: embrace | prompt submitted by @mroutley

#mbsept

Some Day Notes (2023/09/26)

Sep 26, 2023

Without really planning it, the some day note seems to occur about every two weeks. That’s about the right regularity for me without trying too hard, so I’m happy with how that’s working out.

  • Fall! Portland and the PNW has entered fall, both on the calendar but also “on the ground”. Starting this past weekend, the very first atmospheric river of the season came right at us and produced three days of fairly substantial rainfall. I know a lot of people in this country and around the world like to stereotype Portland (and Seattle) as raining all the time, but that simply is not true. And lately with climate change, it is even less true. Measurable rain is anything above 1/10th of an inch. Since April 25, 2023, there have only been five days of measurable rain in Portland. That is five months of basically no rain, not rain all the time, folks. This time of year we actually yearn for rain. We’ve just endured months and months of drought and wildfire, hot weather and dry yards. So I always come back to an essay from about ten years ago written by a guy in Seattle called “Ready For Rain”. It was originally published on Medium, but he seems to have moved it to his own site now as well.
  • I earned my last remaining two Microblog pins: Micro Monday and Night Owl! Completely meaningless, but hey, we have to aim for something in life, right?
  • Before those last two pins, I still needed to earn the pin for Bookshelves, which I wasn’t sure I’d ever get. I read, but it is very sporadic with my illness, trying to keep track of anything I read is like shooting in the dark. My available spoons and ability to withstand visual stress like reading an entire book is not worth it much of the time. However, I was seeking a hummingbird book that I had been trying to get my hands on, and while hoping to find it available on Libby through Multnomah County Library, ended up finding and reading Fastest Things on Wings. What a fulfilling experience that was.
  • I watched all of season two and three (except for the last episode, yet to come out as of this writing) of Reservation Dogs. The episode called “Elora’s Dad” was excellent and I made a small post about it.
  • I deactivated my Twitter account. It was beyond time. The platform is more of a cesspool than it has ever been and rather a security risk. I’m not going to harp on anyone who still uses it but folks really need to be aware of the exposure it puts on you and your information. Since Musk took over, I have had more spam and fraud on my mobile number and associated email address used by that Twitter account than ever before. I know it is from being on that account because I don’t share this number or address with very many people. All of the content you put on there has been leaked and given to bad actors and you better believe it will probably come back to haunt you if they can get something out of it. Musk is a bad man that supports efforts to destroy democracies and support autocracies. You shouldn’t want to be a part of that.
  • I read about Digital Gardens in an older blog post by Maggie Appleton. It is excellent and prompted me to write about what I’m writing about, what I have in progress and want to finish.
  • I joined the Tinylytics webring on weblog and fought with my small-town credit union over the monthly fraud alert saga ensuing from the recurring foreign transaction to pay Tinylytics. Vincent is amazing and has worked with me to get this right and I’m now just going to pay him upfront for a whole year and avoid this headache. If it wasn’t for the grandfathered very low fixed APR on this card I wouldn’t put up with it. But this rate is basically unheard of anymore, and I’ve had the card since I was 18 (30 years).
  • Wordpress now has an ActivityPub plugin to make a blog compatible with the Fediverse and provide comments that will work as replies. I’ve not only discovered that I already have a Wordpress.com account and blog (I never did much with it), but I also have a Wordpress blog on my ancient domain/hosting that I haven’t used since I was in college, yet still pay for. So now I have gears working in my head for another “thing” though not sure what yet. Do I need another blog?! Maybe this is where my newsletter will end up? I’m sure I’ll have more to say on this at some point.

This was a long one, thanks for sticking around. Take care and have a great week!

ü™µ Day 22: road | prompt submitted by @jomalo

#mbsept

Ethan Hawke as Elora's Dad

Sep 21, 2023

I’d like to know the decision matrix that landed on Ethan Hawke as Elora’s dad. Indeed, I did not, have not, had an absent father in my life, though I’ve known many who have. And even more who’ve had a present father of questionable contribution.

But this episode made me cry off and on, throughout. There’s something refreshing and endearing about a father, even an absent one, admitting his every fault that even one that is always present, but can never admit his faults, cannot possess.

It‚Äôs a great moment, at the end of another great episode, near the tail end of a great final season of a great series. Can you believe it‚Äôs all over next week?

No, I can’t.

Finished reading: Fastest Things on Wings

Sep 21, 2023

Finished reading: Fastest Things on Wings by Teresa E. Masear üìö

I suppose I should say something more than what I put in replies on the “Started reading” post for this book. But I’m honestly not sure I could put into words anything better than what the author said is a few paragraphs near the end. It particularly struck me at this time in my life while I look around at a world throwing everything away that we’ve learned about public health, keeping each other safe, and strides we’ve made in protecting the vulnerable, in pursuit of some false freedom and short-sighted sense of “normality”.

We hear this kind of anthropocentric smugness a lot in rehab. Some people insist that individual humans are more important than members of all other species and therefore they do not believe that injured wildlife, even something as wondrous and amazing as a hummingbird, should be rescued. The survival-of-the-fittest argument goes something like this: (1) animals live in nature; (2) nature is a big sorting mechanism that weeds out the weak, which are meant to die, while the strong go on to perpetuate the species; (3) saving the weak propagates a less robust species that ultimately will create the need for more rehab and so on; and, finally, (4) rescuing several hundred hummingbirds every year means nothing in the big scheme of things.

As a young graduate student in my early twenties with no clear career plan, I studied ancient Greek philosophy and spent three years teaching Aristotelian logic to hundreds of exceedingly illogical college undergraduates. Hence my answer to the natural-selection proponents in relation to hummingbird rescue is (1) there is nothing natural about Los Angeles; (2) young hummingbirds who end up in rehab after their nests get cut out of trees to clear the way for an unobstructed view of the city/hillside/ocean whatever are not constitutionally weaker than those born far from such urban threats and don’t deserve to die; (3) some of the strongest, fastest, and most striking birds I have released started out at a disadvantage but nonetheless will produce offspring as physically impressive as any hummingbird on earth; and, finally, (4) next time you are lying in the hospital facing certain death without medical intervention, ask yourself (and this requires brutal honesty) how important your life really is in the big scheme of things. If you have trouble with this last question, try imagining the minuscule planet we inhabit from across the universe, from a vantage point, say, a hundred million light-years away, keeping in mind the immense sweep of history and the billions of humans who have inhabited the earth before you. If, after this philosophical exercise, you are still convinced of your relative importance in the universe, seek help.

Some Day Notes (2023/09/10)

Sep 10, 2023

I think I’ve had some successes over the last few weeks, or at least my brain has the view that I have. That’s gotta be worth something, even if it is erroneous üòÜ

Let’s see what I can come up with on today’s installment of Some Day Notes.

  • I’m participating in the September micro.blog photo challenge. I participated in the March challenge as well, earlier this year, but that was before I “remembered” I had a micro.blog and started using it. In March I relied largely on my library of photos. This time around I am trying to take contemporary photos, while still relying somewhat on my library.
  • I added one of my favorite recipes to weblog, where I occasionally document them with my favorite additions and modifications. This time around it is Creamy Baked Potatoes with a time-tested slew of seasonings that will knock your socks off during bake time, the smell is amazing.
  • I also published a new weblog post, though it is related to a previous post, in that I had intended to expand on a subject mentioned and then forgot. This is about Adding microblogging feeds to weblog.
  • I’ve been revisiting some Agatha Christie adaptations for TV and film. A few of them are what are being referred to as “Alternative History Dramas” that I cannot help but roll my eyes at. At least one of them was worthwhile.
  • I made another trip to visit family last week. It was nothing remarkable but any time I can visit my aging parents is worthwhile in my book. Each time it is made clear that I will need to move closer to them sooner rather than later.
  • We have turned the corner on the seasons in Portland, at least temporarily, as it seems we will get back into the 90s by next weekend. The brief reprieve was nice while it lasted.
  • I’ve been growing my Mastodon accounts. Though not something I purposefully strive for, one of my smaller and more personal accounts that I primarily use for talking about my chronic illness has seen more activity in the last couple weeks than in the previous eight months I’ve had it. Of course, this isn’t a coincidence, as we are in yet another surge of the virus and that always makes content there more abundant and worthy of sharing and talking about. It remains one of the safest spaces on the internet for me.
  • I’ve also been writing some sporadic…poems? They’re more like rhythmic thoughts, but I’m only publishing them to microblog and not the timeline. They’re fairly personal and I’m not even sure how I want to capture them long-term. I will probably need to create a category and page some day.

I guess that’s it for now. Take care and have a great week!

Some Day Notes (2023/08/22)

Aug 22, 2023

I’m still trying to be okay with sharing the good and the bad. It always feels like sharing the bad becomes a turn-off for people and chases them away. This is a sickness in our society. If you’re so fragile that you can’t handle the reality of other people’s pain, well, perhaps you are the one that is not playing your part in this society.

On that note, here is today’s installment of Some Day Notes.

  • We made it through the (latest) heatwave‚ÄîPortland, OR, has now reached and/or exceeded 108¬∞ F four times since records began, all since 2021.
  • I had a meltdown related to my exasperation of trying to survive in a world that doesn’t want me or other chronically ill people to exist. My exasperation on this front continues as the world just digs its collective heels deeper in the sand as more join the chronically ill ranks despite protestations that the virus is over.
  • I visited family on the weekend, something I’ve been trying to keep up with every couple weeks. It’s very hard and takes a lot out of me, most social events do, in addition to the travel time it takes.
  • I completed another micro.blog challenge (post a photo every day for 30 days), though have yet to receive the pin‚Äîapparently it only automatically gets awarded if the photos are “short posts” and a few of mine ended up being long, because words.
  • Wildfires in the NW ensconced the metro area in poor air quality for the last several days. I’ve been holed up in my office cuddling an air purifier and still have a scratchy throat. We are hoping it clears out later today.
  • The noise from one of my neighbors, which I thought was because they had suddenly adopted 4-5 dogs appears to be because they’ve been functioning as a dog-boarding facility at a residential house using an online platform and I suspect they have not gotten licensed nor paid the fees required by Multnomah County. I’ll let one of my other neighbors deal with this if they want to. Seems many are annoyed by the noise and disruption. It’s (not) a lot of fun to have random dogs that don’t live here barking at you while in your own yard.

I’m sure I have more to say, but I’ll leave it at that for now. I haven’t blogged much in more than a week due to “hardships” which have a real toll on my cognition, pain threshold, and ability to write. The heatwave and wildfire smoke haven’t helped either. Really hoping by the next note I’ll have written something, or worked more on my styleguide, or made progress on my micro.blog theme/cross-posting, or my newsletter…or really anything, there’s a lot to do!

Time for some creative lawsuits.

Aug 9, 2023

It seems to me that if SCOTUS deems a web designer’s craft so personal and possessive that they can deny service to a protected class, “ruling that the state of Colorado cannot compel the designer to create work that violates her values,” that same web designer’s craft, or another web designer, or a web developer, etc, should be protected from AI scraping that could end up distorting or using it in a way that “violates her values.”

Honestly, AI in general violates my values.

Remember, the scenario was completely hypothetical. The designer had not even been asked to do this yet. So in the similar manner, a designer being scraped could do this as a hypothetical, I guess. That is, not show standing, as ridiculous as that sounds and is.

Aug 7, 2023

This morning’s gardening video. I enjoy this channel since it is so much different from my climate: east coast/New Jersey, zone 6, and much more humid during the growing season. She’s also got a pretty unique style too and I like mixing it up.

This post to remain name-less.

Aug 6, 2023

There’s a weird curse of growing up GenX. One small part of it for me is the unfortunate recurrence of catching horrific events in our country’s history live on TV. I don’t know if other generations also feel this.

I decided to write up a short list that I can recall tonight, after just watching the season three opener of The West Wing, released in October 2001.

  1. 1986, Challenger Shuttle explosion, my class was watching it live, I was in grade school.
  2. 1999, Columbine HS shooting, I was watching TV at my boyfriend’s apartment and we couldn’t stop watching the coverage after he got home from work. I think this was the first major live-televised coverage of a mass shooting in the US.
  3. 2001, 9/11, my boyfriend had stayed at my house and we got woken up by a call from a friend asking if we’d seen that a plane flew into the World Trade Center, we tuned in after the first plane and watched all day.
  4. 2021, 1/6, I just happened to sit down in front of the TV that morning, as on the west coast it was still early and I decided to see what was on MSNBC around 10am, that was exactly when all the concerned people and whispering started in the chamber and shortly after, well, you know what went down.

I’m sure I am missing some, and maybe I’ll update this post as they come to me. I guess I can just be glad I’m not part of the generation that live-streams the horrific things they are doing to the world.

Reviving the featured photo from the Photos app post.

Jul 21, 2023

I got in the habit of posting the featured photo that my iPad Photos app puts on my home screen widget on one of my Mastodon accounts when I first started on there. I’m actually considering rolling that account in favor of my micro.blog account with custom domain.

It was my first Mastodon account and I hadn’t done much instance research other than to “follow” one of my favorite people from Twitter there. It’s a big instance and what I’ve found I like most about Mastodon and other Fediverse-type services is the “local” or “community” aspect. I’ve even now found that bringing some of those people from other instances into that community can sometimes end up with them moving their account to another instance too. It’s an interesting ecosystem. I like the flexibility. It’s too bad you lose post history, though.

Anyway, before officially deciding on the account merge/switch/rollover, I’ve decided to revive the featured photo tradition here, starting today.

The stream of consciousness timeline.

Jul 18, 2023

I suppose I am still addicted or accustomed to the stream of consciousness chronological social media timeline, and less of a curated or refined timeline. As I’m testing this out, I still seem to spend my day in Mastodon while working, and then pop in and out of micro.blog during “lulls” in the day. Or like tonight, while I’m watching TV.

Weblog will probably continue to be where I put “real thought” into the content and share something I want around for the longer term or for reference over time. I still really want to make this be the launchpad for that stream of consciousness, but use filters + categories to ensure they get to the desired outlet rather than just spewing everywhere.

It might be a bit choppy for a while until I find a rhythm. And then I’ll change it up again when I decide to get moving on the newsletter.

Edit: I used the wrong word/misspelling (conscience v consciousness) three times in this post üôÑ

Phantom posts, batch edit/delete, category woes.

Jul 17, 2023

I do keep having some phantom posts show up from feeds that I have since removed and deleted the entries for on micro.blog. I do not recommend this to anyone or myself in the future. Perhaps there is a batch process I’m missing for deleting or adding categories to multiple posts at a time. That would be very useful.

I assume this is a cache issue and will resolve itself, but I’m caught off-guard every time I refresh and some old since-deleted post re-emerges. If I manually trigger a rebuild they go away.

BTW, I can see that having both a Mastodon and Microblog category right next to each other is going to be an issue, since I keep messing up the one I apply.

Is there another

Jul 17, 2023

…for discovery other than Discover or using one of the emoji filters? Something more chronological yet timely. It seems that Discover is a few days behind, or is it manually curated?

Local is actually one of the things I like most for discovering new people on an instance I just joined or for finding a new instance that I might want to be on. I know this isn’t an “instance” of Mastodon, but considering I’m here already and know I want to be here, I was hoping for just general stream of conscience from micro.blog. Perhaps I need to get that from a feed somehow.
